Cosine and Secant are positive. * *Mnemonic:* "All Students Take Calculus" or "Add Sugar To Coffee" (reading counter-clockwise from Q1). * **Example:** For $\theta = 150^\circ$ (Quadrant II): * The point on the unit circle would have x-coordinate negative and y-coordinate positive. * $\sin 150^\circ > 0$, $\cos 150^\circ < 0$, $\tan 150^\circ < 0$. --- ### 5. Reference Angles: The Key Concept * **Definition:** The reference angle ($\theta_R$) for an angle $\theta$ in standard position is the **acute angle** (between 0° and 90° or 0 and $\pi/2$ radians) formed by the terminal side of $\theta$ and the **x-axis**. * **Why are they useful?** They allow us to evaluate trigonometric functions for *any* angle by relating them back to the familiar values of acute angles. The magnitude of the trig function for $\theta$ is the same as for its reference angle; only the sign changes depending on the quadrant. * **How to find Reference Angles ($\theta_R$):** 1. **If $\theta$ is in Quadrant I (0° < $\theta$ < 90°):** * $\theta_R = \theta$ * *Example:* For $\theta = 45^\circ$, $\theta_R = 45^\circ$. 2. **If $\theta$ is in Quadrant II (90° < $\theta$ < 180°):** * $\theta_R = 180^\circ - \theta$ (or $\pi - \theta$ in radians) * *Example:* For $\theta = 150^\circ$, $\theta_R = 180^\circ - 150^\circ = 30^\circ$. 3. **If $\theta$ is in Quadrant III (180° < $\theta$ < 270°):** * $\theta_R = \theta - 180^\circ$ (or $\theta - \pi$ in radians) * *Example:* For $\theta = 210^\circ$, $\theta_R = 210^\circ - 180^\circ = 30^\circ$. 4. **If $\theta$ is in Quadrant IV (270° < $\theta$ < 360°):** * $\theta_R = 360^\circ - \theta$ (or $2\pi - \theta$ in radians) * *Example:* For $\theta = 315^\circ$, $\theta_R = 360^\circ - 315^\circ = 45^\circ$. * **For angles outside 0° to 360°:** First, find a coterminal angle within this range by adding or subtracting multiples of 360° (or $2\pi$). Then apply the rules above. * *Example:* For $\theta = 400^\circ$, a coterminal angle is $400^\circ - 360^\circ = 40^\circ$. Since $40^\circ$ is in Q1, $\theta_R = 40^\circ$. * *Example:* For $\theta = -120^\circ$, a coterminal angle is $-120^\circ + 360^\circ = 240^\circ$. $240^\circ$ is in Q3, so $\theta_R = 240^\circ - 180^\circ = 60^\circ$. --- ### 6. Using Reference Angles to Evaluate Trigonometric Functions This is a two-step process: 1. **Determine the reference angle** for the given angle $\theta$. 2. **Determine the sign** of the trigonometric function based on the quadrant of $\theta$ (using the ASTC rule). 3. **Evaluate the trigonometric function** for the reference angle, and apply the correct sign. * **Common Angle Values (Memorize/Derive):** | Angle ($\theta$) | Radians | $\sin \theta$ | $\cos \theta$ | $\tan \theta$ | | :---------------- | :------ | :------------ | :------------ | :------------ | | 0° | 0 | 0 | 1 | 0 | | 30° | $\pi/6$ | 1/2 | $\sqrt{3}/2$ | $1/\sqrt{3}$ | | 45° | $\pi/4$ | $\sqrt{2}/2$ | $\sqrt{2}/2$ | 1 | | 60° | $\pi/3$ | $\sqrt{3}/2$ | 1/2 | $\sqrt{3}$ | | 90° | $\pi/2$ | 1 | 0 | Undefined | * **Derivations for special angles:** * **45-45-90 Triangle:** In a unit circle, for $\theta=45^\circ$, x=y. Since $x^2+y^2=1$, $2x^2=1 \implies x = 1/\sqrt{2} = \sqrt{2}/2$. So, $\sin 45^\circ = \cos 45^\circ = \sqrt{2}/2$. * **30-60-90 Triangle:** Consider an equilateral triangle with side length 1. Drop an altitude, which bisects the base and the top angle. This creates two 30-60-90 triangles with hypotenuse 1, opposite side to 30° is 1/2, and adjacent side to 30° is $\sqrt{1^2-(1/2)^2} = \sqrt{3}/2$. * Thus, $\sin 30^\circ = 1/2$, $\cos 30^\circ = \sqrt{3}/2$. * And $\sin 60^\circ = \sqrt{3}/2$, $\cos 60^\circ = 1/2$. * **Examples:** 1. **Evaluate $\sin(210^\circ)$:** * **Step 1: Quadrant and Reference Angle.** $210^\circ$ is in Quadrant III. * $\theta_R = 210^\circ - 180^\circ = 30^\circ$. * **Step 2: Sign.** In Quadrant III, sine is negative. * **Step 3: Evaluate.** $\sin(210^\circ) = -\sin(30^\circ) = -1/2$. 2. **Evaluate $\cos(7\pi/4)$:** * **Step 1: Quadrant and Reference Angle.** $7\pi/4$ is equivalent to $315^\circ$ (since $\pi/4 = 45^\circ$, $7 \times 45^\circ = 315^\circ$). This is in Quadrant IV. * $\theta_R = 2\pi - 7\pi/4 = 8\pi/4 - 7\pi/4 = \pi/4$. * **Step 2: Sign.** In Quadrant IV, cosine is positive. * **Step 3: Evaluate.** $\cos(7\pi/4) = +\cos(\pi/4) = \sqrt{2}/2$. 3. **Evaluate $\tan(-120^\circ)$:** * **Step 1: Coterminal Angle.** $-120^\circ + 360^\circ = 240^\circ$. * **Step 2: Quadrant and Reference Angle.** $240^\circ$ is in Quadrant III. * $\theta_R = 240^\circ - 180^\circ = 60^\circ$. * **Step 3: Sign.** In Quadrant III, tangent is positive. * **Step 4: Evaluate.** $\tan(-120^\circ) = +\tan(60^\circ) = \sqrt{3}$. --- ### 7. Applications and Further Concepts * **Periodic Nature:** The unit circle explicitly demonstrates the periodic nature of trigonometric functions. Since angles repeat every 360° (or $2\pi$ radians), the values of $\sin \theta$ and $\cos \theta$ also repeat. $\sin(\theta + 2\pi n) = \sin \theta$ and $\cos(\theta + 2\pi n) = \cos \theta$ for any integer n. This is fundamental to wave analysis. * **Solving Trigonometric Equations:** Reference angles are critical for finding all possible solutions to trigonometric equations, as they help identify angles in different quadrants that yield the same absolute value. * **Connection to Complex Numbers (Interesting Point for Engineering Students):** The unit circle forms the basis for Euler's formula, a cornerstone in electrical engineering, signal processing, and quantum mechanics: * $e^{i\theta} = \cos \theta + i \sin \theta$ * Here, 'i' is the imaginary unit. This formula elegantly connects exponential functions, complex numbers, and trigonometry, providing a powerful tool for representing sinusoidal signals and phasors. Points on the unit circle can be viewed as complex numbers with magnitude 1. * **Phasors and AC Circuits:** In AC circuit analysis, sinusoidal voltages and currents are often represented as phasors, which are complex numbers that can be visualized as rotating vectors on a complex plane (which is essentially a unit circle for normalized values). --- ### 8. Summary of Key Points * The **Unit Circle** is a circle of radius 1 centered at the origin, extending trigonometric definitions to all angles. * For any angle $\theta$, the point (x,y) on the unit circle's terminal side corresponds to $x = \cos \theta$ and $y = \sin \theta$. * Angles in **Standard Position** have their vertex at the origin and initial side on the positive x-axis. Angles can be positive (counter-clockwise) or negative (clockwise). * **Coterminal angles** share the same terminal side and differ by multiples of 360° or $2\pi$ radians. * **Radians** are the natural unit for angles in higher mathematics and engineering, simplifying calculus formulas. * Trigonometric functions are redefined using x and y coordinates on the unit circle, with their signs determined by the **quadrant** (ASTC rule). * A **Reference Angle** is the acute angle formed by the terminal side and the x-axis, used to simplify calculations by relating any angle's trig values to acute angle values. * To evaluate trig functions for any angle: find its reference angle, determine the sign based on its quadrant, and apply the known value for the reference angle. * The unit circle highlights the **periodic nature** of trigonometric functions and is fundamental to advanced concepts like complex numbers (Euler's formula) and their engineering applications. 6.) Trigonometric Functions of Any Angle **Trigonometric Functions of Any Angle** Traditionally, trigonometry is introduced using right-angled triangles, defining sine, cosine, and tangent for acute angles (0° to 90°) based on ratios of sides (SOH CAH TOA). While foundational, this approach is limited to angles within a right triangle. To understand periodic phenomena, waves, and rotational motion prevalent in engineering, we need to define trigonometric functions for *any* angle – positive, negative, and angles greater than 360°. This generalization is achieved through the **Unit Circle**. --- ### 1. The Need for Generalization: Beyond Right Triangles * **Right-Triangle Definition Recap:** * For an acute angle $\theta$ in a right triangle: * $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ * $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ * $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$ * **Limitation:** This definition only applies to angles strictly between 0° and 90° (or 0 and $\pi/2$ radians), as angles in a right triangle must be acute. How do we define $\sin(150^\circ)$ or $\cos(-45^\circ)$? The answer lies in the unit circle. --- ### 2. Generalizing Angles: Standard Position and the Unit Circle To extend trigonometric definitions, we introduce the concept of an angle in **standard position** and the **unit circle**. * **Angle in Standard Position:** * An angle $\theta$ is in standard position when its vertex is at the origin (0,0) of a Cartesian coordinate system, and its initial side lies along the positive x-axis. * The terminal side is the ray that rotates around the origin. * **Positive Angles:** Formed by counter-clockwise rotation from the positive x-axis. * **Negative Angles:** Formed by clockwise rotation from the positive x-axis. * **Example:** $90^\circ$ (counter-clockwise to positive y-axis), $-90^\circ$ (clockwise to negative y-axis), $450^\circ$ (one full rotation + $90^\circ$). * **Coterminal Angles:** * Angles that share the same initial side and terminal side are called coterminal angles. * They differ by an integer multiple of $360^\circ$ (or $2\pi$ radians). * **Formula:** If $\theta$ is an angle, then $\theta + n \cdot 360^\circ$ (or $\theta + n \cdot 2\pi$ radians) for any integer $n$ represents angles coterminal with $\theta$. * **Example:** $30^\circ$, $390^\circ$ ($30^\circ + 360^\circ$), and $-330^\circ$ ($30^\circ - 360^\circ$) are all coterminal. They all have the same terminal side. * **The Unit Circle:** * A circle with its center at the origin (0,0) and a radius of 1 unit. * Let $P(x,y)$ be any point on the unit circle. If an angle $\theta$ in standard position has its terminal side passing through $P(x,y)$, then: * The x-coordinate of $P$ is defined as $\cos(\theta)$. * The y-coordinate of $P$ is defined as $\sin(\theta)$. * Thus, $P(x,y) = (\cos(\theta), \sin(\theta))$. * **Derivation/Proof:** Consider the point $P(x,y)$ on the unit circle. Drop a perpendicular from $P$ to the x-axis, forming a right triangle (if $\theta$ is not on an axis). The hypotenuse is the radius, which is 1. The adjacent side is $x$, and the opposite side is $y$. * $\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}} = \frac{x}{1} = x$ * $\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}} = \frac{y}{1} = y$ * $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}} = \frac{y}{x}$ (provided $x \neq 0$) * This definition is universal; it works for *any* angle because $x$ and $y$ can be positive, negative, or zero. --- ### 3. Trigonometric Functions in All Four Quadrants The signs of sine, cosine, and tangent depend on the quadrant in which the terminal side of the angle lies. * **Quadrant I (QI):** $0^\circ < \theta < 90^\circ$ * $x > 0$, $y > 0$. * $\sin(\theta) = y$ (positive) * $\cos(\theta) = x$ (positive) * $\tan(\theta) = y/x$ (positive) * *All* trig functions are positive. * **Quadrant II (QII):** $90^\circ < \theta < 180^\circ$ * $x < 0$, $y > 0$. * $\sin(\theta) = y$ (positive) * $\cos(\theta) = x$ (negative) * $\tan(\theta) = y/x$ (negative) * Only $\sin(\theta)$ (and its reciprocal $\csc(\theta)$) is positive. * **Quadrant III (QIII):** $180^\circ < \theta < 270^\circ$ * $x < 0$, $y < 0$. * $\sin(\theta) = y$ (negative) * $\cos(\theta) = x$ (negative) * $\tan(\theta) = y/x$ (positive) * Only $\tan(\theta)$ (and its reciprocal $\cot(\theta)$) is positive. * **Quadrant IV (QIV):** $270^\circ < \theta < 360^\circ$ * $x > 0$, $y < 0$. * $\sin(\theta) = y$ (negative) * $\cos(\theta) = x$ (positive) * $\tan(\theta) = y/x$ (negative) * Only $\cos(\theta)$ (and its reciprocal $\sec(\theta)$) is positive. * **Mnemonic (CAST Rule):** * To remember the signs, use the "CAST" rule, starting from QIV and going counter-clockwise: * **C** (Quadrant IV): Cosine positive * **A** (Quadrant I): All positive * **S** (Quadrant II): Sine positive * **T** (Quadrant III): Tangent positive --- ### 4. Reference Angles A **reference angle** ($\theta'$) is the acute angle formed by the terminal side of an angle $\theta$ and the x-axis. It is always positive. * **Purpose:** To find the values of trigonometric functions for any angle, we can use its reference angle and then apply the appropriate sign based on the quadrant. * **How to find $\theta'$:** * **QI:** $\theta' = \theta$ * **QII:** $\theta' = 180^\circ - \theta$ (or $\pi - \theta$ radians) * **QIII:** $\theta' = \theta - 180^\circ$ (or $\theta - \pi$ radians) * **QIV:** $\theta' = 360^\circ - \theta$ (or $2\pi - \theta$ radians) * For angles outside $0^\circ$ to $360^\circ$, first find a coterminal angle within this range. * **Example:** * Find $\sin(210^\circ)$. 1. $210^\circ$ is in QIII. 2. Reference angle $\theta' = 210^\circ - 180^\circ = 30^\circ$. 3. In QIII, sine is negative (CAST rule). 4. So, $\sin(210^\circ) = -\sin(30^\circ) = -1/2$. --- ### 5. Special Angles Certain angles have exact trigonometric values that are essential to know. These come from the properties of 30-60-90 and 45-45-90 right triangles, or directly from the unit circle. * **Key Angles (Degrees/Radians) and their Unit Circle Coordinates (x,y) = (cos, sin):** * $0^\circ / 0$: $(1, 0)$ * $30^\circ / \pi/6$: $(\sqrt{3}/2, 1/2)$ * $45^\circ / \pi/4$: $(\sqrt{2}/2, \sqrt{2}/2)$ * $60^\circ / \pi/3$: $(1/2, \sqrt{3}/2)$ * $90^\circ / \pi/2$: $(0, 1)$ * $180^\circ / \pi$: $(-1, 0)$ * $270^\circ / 3\pi/2$: $(0, -1)$ * $360^\circ / 2\pi$: $(1, 0)$ (same as $0^\circ$) * **Example using $120^\circ$:** * $120^\circ$ is in QII. Reference angle is $180^\circ - 120^\circ = 60^\circ$. * In QII, $\sin$ is positive, $\cos$ is negative, $\tan$ is negative. * $\sin(120^\circ) = \sin(60^\circ) = \sqrt{3}/2$ * $\cos(120^\circ) = -\cos(60^\circ) = -1/2$ * $\tan(120^\circ) = -\tan(60^\circ) = -\sqrt{3}$ --- ### 6. Reciprocal Trigonometric Functions In addition to sine, cosine, and tangent, there are three reciprocal functions: * **Cosecant (csc):** Reciprocal of sine. * $\csc(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)} = \frac{1}{y}$ * Undefined when $\sin(\theta) = 0$ (i.e., at $0^\circ, \pm 180^\circ, \pm 360^\circ, \dots$). * **Secant (sec):** Reciprocal of cosine. * $\sec(\theta) = \frac{1}{\cos(\theta)} = \frac{1}{x}$ * Undefined when $\cos(\theta) = 0$ (i.e., at $\pm 90^\circ, \pm 270^\circ, \dots$). * **Cotangent (cot):** Reciprocal of tangent. * $\cot(\theta) = \frac{1}{\tan(\theta)} = \frac{x}{y}$ * Undefined when $\tan(\theta) = 0$ (i.e., at $0^\circ, \pm 180^\circ, \pm 360^\circ, \dots$). * Also can be defined as $\cot(\theta) = \frac{\cos(\theta)}{\sin(\theta)}$. --- ### 7. Domain and Range of Trigonometric Functions Understanding the domain (possible input angles) and range (possible output values) is crucial. * **Sine and Cosine:** * **Domain:** All real numbers ($-\infty < \theta < \infty$). You can take the sine or cosine of any angle. * **Range:** $[-1, 1]$. Since $x$ and $y$ are coordinates on the unit circle (radius 1), their values cannot exceed 1 or be less than -1. * **Tangent:** * **Domain:** All real numbers except angles where $\cos(\theta)=0$. * These are odd multiples of $90^\circ$ or $\pi/2$ radians: $\theta \neq \frac{\pi}{2} + n\pi$, where $n$ is an integer. * **Range:** All real numbers ($-\infty < \tan(\theta) < \infty$). * **Interesting Point:** As $\theta$ approaches $90^\circ$ or $270^\circ$, the x-coordinate approaches 0, causing $y/x$ to approach $\pm \infty$. * **Cosecant:** * **Domain:** All real numbers except angles where $\sin(\theta)=0$. * These are integer multiples of $180^\circ$ or $\pi$ radians: $\theta \neq n\pi$, where $n$ is an integer. * **Range:** $(-\infty, -1] \cup [1, \infty)$. Since $\sin(\theta) \in [-1, 1]$, its reciprocal $\csc(\theta)$ must be outside $(-1, 1)$. * **Secant:** * **Domain:** All real numbers except angles where $\cos(\theta)=0$. * These are odd multiples of $90^\circ$ or $\pi/2$ radians: $\theta \neq \frac{\pi}{2} + n\pi$, where $n$ is an integer. * **Range:** $(-\infty, -1] \cup [1, \infty)$. Similar to cosecant. * **Cotangent:** * **Domain:** All real numbers except angles where $\sin(\theta)=0$. * These are integer multiples of $180^\circ$ or $\pi$ radians: $\theta \neq n\pi$, where $n$ is an integer. * **Range:** All real numbers ($-\infty < \cot(\theta) < \infty$). --- ### 8. Fundamental Trigonometric Identities These identities are derived directly from the definitions and the Pythagorean theorem on the unit circle. They are crucial for simplifying expressions and solving equations. * **Reciprocal Identities:** * $\csc(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)}$ * $\sec(\theta) = \frac{1}{\cos(\theta)}$ * $\cot(\theta) = \frac{1}{\tan(\theta)}$ * **Quotient Identities:** * $\tan(\theta) = \frac{\sin(\theta)}{\cos(\theta)}$ * $\cot(\theta) = \frac{\cos(\theta)}{\sin(\theta)}$ * **Pythagorean Identities:** These are arguably the most important. * **1. $\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$** * **Derivation/Proof:** * Consider a point $P(x,y)$ on the unit circle. The distance from the origin to $P$ is the radius, $r=1$. * By the Pythagorean theorem, $x^2 + y^2 = r^2$. * Since $x = \cos(\theta)$ and $y = \sin(\theta)$ on the unit circle, and $r=1$: * $(\cos(\theta))^2 + (\sin(\theta))^2 = 1^2$ * $\cos^2(\theta) + \sin^2(\theta) = 1$ (This is why it's called a Pythagorean identity!) * **2. $1 + \tan^2(\theta) = \sec^2(\theta)$** * **Derivation:** Divide the first identity by $\cos^2(\theta)$ (assuming $\cos(\theta) \neq 0$): * $\frac{\sin^2(\theta)}{\cos^2(\theta)} + \frac{\cos^2(\theta)}{\cos^2(\theta)} = \frac{1}{\cos^2(\theta)}$ * $\left(\frac{\sin(\theta)}{\cos(\theta)}\right)^2 + 1 = \left(\frac{1}{\cos(\theta)}\right)^2$ * $\tan^2(\theta) + 1 = \sec^2(\theta)$ * **3. $1 + \cot^2(\theta) = \csc^2(\theta)$** * **Derivation:** Divide the first identity by $\sin^2(\theta)$ (assuming $\sin(\theta) \neq 0$): * $\frac{\sin^2(\theta)}{\sin^2(\theta)} + \frac{\cos^2(\theta)}{\sin^2(\theta)} = \frac{1}{\sin^2(\theta)}$ * $1 + \left(\frac{\cos(\theta)}{\sin(\theta)}\right)^2 = \left(\frac{1}{\sin(\theta)}\right)^2$ * $1 + \cot^2(\theta) = \csc^2(\theta)$ --- ### 9. Periodicity of Trigonometric Functions A function is **periodic** if its values repeat at regular intervals. Trigonometric functions are inherently periodic due to their connection to circular motion. * **Period of Sine and Cosine:** * The period of $\sin(\theta)$ and $\cos(\theta)$ is $360^\circ$ or $2\pi$ radians. * This means $\sin(\theta) = \sin(\theta + 360^\circ n)$ and $\cos(\theta) = \cos(\theta + 360^\circ n)$ for any integer $n$. * This directly follows from the concept of coterminal angles: adding or subtracting full rotations brings you to the same terminal side, hence the same $(x,y)$ coordinates. * **Period of Tangent and Cotangent:** * The period of $\tan(\theta)$ and $\cot(\theta)$ is $180^\circ$ or $\pi$ radians. * This means $\tan(\theta) = \tan(\theta + 180^\circ n)$ and $\cot(\theta) = \cot(\theta + 180^\circ n)$ for any integer $n$. * **Interesting Point:** While $\sin$ and $\cos$ repeat every $2\pi$, $\tan$ repeats every $\pi$. This is because $\tan(\theta) = y/x$. When you rotate by $180^\circ$, both $x$ and $y$ change sign (e.g., $(x,y)$ becomes $(-x,-y)$). The ratio $\frac{-y}{-x} = \frac{y}{x}$ remains the same. * **Period of Cosecant and Secant:** * The period of $\csc(\theta)$ and $\sec(\theta)$ is $360^\circ$ or $2\pi$ radians, as they are reciprocals of sine and cosine, respectively. --- ### 10. Graphs of Trigonometric Functions (Brief Visualisation) While not a direct topic of "functions of any angle," understanding the graphs visually reinforces the concepts of domain, range, and periodicity: * **Sine and Cosine Waves:** Smooth, continuous waves oscillating between -1 and 1, repeating every $2\pi$ radians. * **Tangent Wave:** Has vertical asymptotes where $\cos(\theta)=0$ (odd multiples of $\pi/2$), and values span from $-\infty$ to $\infty$, repeating every $\pi$ radians. --- ### 11. Applications in Engineering (Extra Interesting Point) The generalization of trigonometric functions to any angle is fundamental to various engineering fields: * **Electrical Engineering:** Analyzing alternating current (AC) circuits, signal processing (Fourier series/transforms use sine and cosine waves to decompose complex signals). * **Mechanical Engineering:** Describing oscillatory motion (e.g., pendulums, vibrations in structures), rotational kinematics (angular displacement, velocity, acceleration). * **Civil Engineering:** Structural analysis involving forces at various angles, wave dynamics (water waves, seismic waves). * **Robotics:** Kinematics (position and orientation of robot arms) heavily rely on rotations described by trigonometric functions. * **Computer Graphics:** Transformations (rotations) in 2D and 3D space. --- ### Summary of Key Points: * Trigonometric functions are generalized for **any angle** (positive, negative, greater than 360°) using the **Unit Circle**. * An angle in **standard position** has its vertex at the origin and initial side on the positive x-axis. * For a point $P(x,y)$ on the unit circle corresponding to angle $\theta$, $\cos(\theta) = x$ and $\sin(\theta) = y$. $\tan(\theta) = y/x$. * The **CAST rule** helps determine the signs of trig functions in each quadrant. * **Reference angles** simplify evaluation by relating any angle to an acute angle in Q1. * **Reciprocal functions** are $\csc(\theta) = 1/\sin(\theta)$, $\sec(\theta) = 1/\cos(\theta)$, $\cot(\theta) = 1/\tan(\theta)$. * **Domain and Range** specify valid inputs and outputs, with tangent, cotangent, secant, and cosecant having restricted domains due to division by zero. * **Pythagorean Identities** ($\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$, etc.) are fundamental and derived from the unit circle. * **Periodicity** means function values repeat: $2\pi$ for sin, cos, csc, sec; $\pi$ for tan, cot. * These concepts are vital for modeling periodic phenomena and rotational motion in various engineering disciplines. 7.) Graphs of Trigonometric Functions (Amplitude ## Graphs of Trigonometric Functions: Understanding Amplitude Trigonometric functions are fundamental in engineering and science, describing periodic phenomena like waves, oscillations, and rotations. Understanding their graphs, especially key parameters like amplitude, is crucial for modeling and analyzing these systems. ### 1. Introduction to Trigonometric Functions and Their Graphs Trigonometric functions (sine, cosine, tangent, cosecant, secant, cotangent) are ratios of sides of a right-angled triangle, defined in the context of the unit circle, and extended to real numbers. Their periodic nature means their graphs repeat over a fixed interval. * **Sine Function ($y = \sin(x)$):** * **Domain:** All real numbers ($\mathbb{R}$). * **Range:** $[-1, 1]$. * **Period:** $2\pi$. * Starts at (0,0), goes up to a max of 1, down to 0, then to a min of -1, back to 0. * **Cosine Function ($y = \cos(x)$):** * **Domain:** All real numbers ($\mathbb{R}$). * **Range:** $[-1, 1]$. * **Period:** $2\pi$. * Starts at (0,1), goes down to 0, then to a min of -1, up to 0, back to 1. Graphing these functions helps us visualize their behavior, identify their maximum and minimum values, and understand how various parameters transform their basic shapes. ### 2. Key Properties of Trigonometric Graphs When we transform the basic sine or cosine function from $y = \sin(x)$ or $y = \cos(x)$ to a more general form like $y = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$ or $y = A \cos(Bx + C) + D$, four main parameters affect the graph: * **Amplitude (|A|):** Controls the vertical stretch or compression of the graph, determining the maximum displacement from the midline. * **Period (2π/|B|):** Determines the length of one complete cycle of the graph. * **Phase Shift (-C/B):** Controls the horizontal shift (left or right) of the graph. * **Vertical Shift (D):** Controls the vertical shift (up or down) of the graph, establishing the midline. Our focus today is exclusively on **Amplitude**. ### 3. Deep Dive: Amplitude #### 3.1. Definition The **amplitude** of a periodic function is the maximum absolute value of its displacement from the function's midline (or equilibrium position). For standard sine and cosine waves, the midline is the x-axis ($y=0$). * **Analogy:** Imagine a pendulum swinging. The amplitude is the maximum distance the pendulum bob moves from its resting (vertical) position, either to the left or to the right. It's the "height" of the swing. #### 3.2. Mathematical Representation For trigonometric functions of the form: $y = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$ $y = A \cos(Bx + C) + D$ The **amplitude** is given by the absolute value of the coefficient $A$, denoted as **$|A|$**. **Why absolute value?** Amplitude represents a physical distance or magnitude, like the height of a wave or the loudness of a sound. Distances and magnitudes are always non-negative. A negative value for $A$ (e.g., $A = -2$) doesn't mean the wave has negative amplitude; it means the wave is inverted or reflected across its midline, but its "height" from the midline is still positive ($|-2| = 2$). #### 3.3. Impact of 'A' on the Graph The value of $A$ (specifically $|A|$) vertically scales the graph: * **If $|A| > 1$ (e.g., $A=3$):** The graph is **vertically stretched**. The peaks (maximum values) and troughs (minimum values) are farther away from the midline. The waves appear taller. * Example: $y = 3 \sin(x)$. The maximum value will be $3 \times 1 = 3$, and the minimum value will be $3 \times (-1) = -3$. The range is $[-3, 3]$. * **If $0 < |A| < 1$ (e.g., $A=0.5$):** The graph is **vertically compressed** (or squashed). The peaks and troughs are closer to the midline. The waves appear shorter. * Example: $y = 0.5 \cos(x)$. The maximum value will be $0.5 \times 1 = 0.5$, and the minimum value will be $0.5 \times (-1) = -0.5$. The range is $[-0.5, 0.5]$. * **If $A < 0$ (e.g., $A=-1$ or $A=-2$):** In addition to the vertical stretch/compression based on $|A|$, the graph is **reflected across its midline** (usually the x-axis if no vertical shift). The peaks of the original function become troughs, and the troughs become peaks. The amplitude, however, remains $|A|$. * Example: $y = -2 \sin(x)$. The amplitude is $|-2| = 2$. * For $y = \sin(x)$, max is 1, min is -1. * For $y = -2 \sin(x)$, the maximum value will be $-2 \times (-1) = 2$, and the minimum value will be $-2 \times 1 = -2$. The range is $[-2, 2]$. Notice that the original peaks are now low points relative to the x-axis, and original troughs are now high points. #### 3.4. Derivation/Proof: Why Amplitude is $|A|$ Let's consider the general form $y = A \sin(X)$, where $X$ can represent any angle (like $Bx+C$). We know that for the basic sine function, $-1 \le \sin(X) \le 1$. **Case 1: $A > 0$** (e.g., $A=3$) If we multiply the inequality by a positive number $A$, the inequality signs do not change: $A \times (-1) \le A \times \sin(X) \le A \times 1$ $-A \le A \sin(X) \le A$ So, the maximum value of $y$ is $A$, and the minimum value of $y$ is $-A$. The amplitude is defined as half the difference between the maximum and minimum values: Amplitude = $\frac{\text{Maximum Value} - \text{Minimum Value}}{2}$ Amplitude = $\frac{A - (-A)}{2} = \frac{2A}{2} = A$. Since $A>0$, this is equal to $|A|$. **Case 2: $A < 0$** (e.g., $A=-2$) If we multiply the inequality by a negative number $A$, the inequality signs *reverse*: $-1 \le \sin(X) \le 1$ $A \times (-1) \ge A \times \sin(X) \ge A \times 1$ $-A \ge A \sin(X) \ge A$ To write this in standard increasing order (minimum to maximum): $A \le A \sin(X) \le -A$ So, the maximum value of $y$ is $-A$, and the minimum value of $y$ is $A$. Amplitude = $\frac{\text{Maximum Value} - \text{Minimum Value}}{2}$ Amplitude = $\frac{-A - A}{2} = \frac{-2A}{2} = -A$. Since $A$ is a negative number (e.g., $A=-2$), $-A$ will be a positive number (e.g., $-(-2)=2$). Therefore, $-A = |A|$. **Conclusion:** In both cases ($A>0$ and $A<0$), the amplitude is always $|A|$. #### 3.5. Examples Let's illustrate with various transformations: 1. **$y = 5 \sin(x)$** * $A = 5$. Amplitude = $|5| = 5$. * The graph will oscillate between -5 and 5. 2. **$y = -1.5 \cos(x)$** * $A = -1.5$. Amplitude = $|-1.5| = 1.5$. * The graph will oscillate between -1.5 and 1.5, but it will be reflected across the x-axis compared to $y = 1.5 \cos(x)$. 3. **$y = 2 \sin(3x)$** * $A = 2$. Amplitude = $|2| = 2$. * (Note: The '3' affects the period, not the amplitude.) * The graph oscillates between -2 and 2. 4. **$y = 4 \cos(\pi x - \pi/2) + 1$** * $A = 4$. Amplitude = $|4| = 4$. * (Note: The '+1' is a vertical shift, and the $\pi x - \pi/2$ affects period and phase shift.) * The midline for this function is $y=1$. The graph will oscillate 4 units above and 4 units below this midline. So, its maximum value will be $1 + 4 = 5$, and its minimum value will be $1 - 4 = -3$. The range is $[-3, 5]$. #### 3.6. Real-World Applications Amplitude plays a crucial role in describing the "strength" or "intensity" of many natural and engineered phenomena: * **Sound Waves:** The amplitude of a sound wave corresponds to its **loudness** or intensity. A larger amplitude means a louder sound. * **Light Waves:** The amplitude of an electromagnetic wave (like light) is related to its **brightness** or intensity. Higher amplitude means brighter light. * **Mechanical Oscillations:** * **Spring-Mass Systems:** For a mass oscillating on a spring, the amplitude is the maximum displacement from the equilibrium position. It's directly related to the energy stored in the oscillation. * **Pendulums:** The amplitude of a pendulum swing is the maximum angular displacement from the vertical. * **Alternating Current (AC) Circuits:** The amplitude of an AC voltage or current describes its peak value. For example, household voltage is often described as 120V AC, which is an RMS value, but its peak amplitude is closer to 170V. * **Tides:** The amplitude of ocean tides refers to half the difference between high tide and low tide, indicating the strength of the tidal pull. * **Earthquakes:** The amplitude of seismic waves is a measure of the ground displacement, contributing to the perceived intensity of the earthquake. ### 4. How to Find Amplitude from a Graph If you are given a graph of a trigonometric function, you can determine its amplitude by following these steps: 1. **Identify the Maximum Value ($y_{max}$):** Find the highest point the graph reaches. 2. **Identify the Minimum Value ($y_{min}$):** Find the lowest point the graph reaches. 3. **Calculate the Amplitude:** Use the formula: Amplitude = $\frac{y_{max} - y_{min}}{2}$ **Example:** Suppose a graph has a maximum value of 7 and a minimum value of 1. Amplitude = $\frac{7 - 1}{2} = \frac{6}{2} = 3$. **Extra Point: Finding the Midline** While not directly amplitude, knowing the midline helps visualize the amplitude. The midline is the horizontal line exactly halfway between the maximum and minimum values. Midline (Vertical Shift $D$) = $\frac{y_{max} + y_{min}}{2}$ For the example above, Midline = $\frac{7 + 1}{2} = \frac{8}{2} = 4$. So, the function would be of the form $y = A \sin(Bx+C) + 4$ with amplitude $|A|=3$. ### 5. Interesting Points and Advanced Considerations * **Damped Oscillations:** In many real-world systems, oscillations don't continue indefinitely with constant amplitude. Due to energy loss (e.g., friction, air resistance), the amplitude gradually decreases over time. This is modeled by functions where the amplitude itself is a decaying exponential function, like $y = Ae^{-kt} \cos(Bx)$. Here, the amplitude is $Ae^{-kt}$, which diminishes as $t$ increases. This is critical in mechanical and electrical engineering. * **Modulated Waves:** Sometimes, the amplitude of one wave is varied by another signal. This is called amplitude modulation (AM) and is a fundamental concept in radio communication. The carrier wave's amplitude is changed in proportion to the information signal. * **Energy Relation:** In many physical systems (e.g., vibrating strings, quantum mechanics), the energy associated with a wave or oscillation is proportional to the square of its amplitude ($E \propto A^2$). This highlights the significance of amplitude as a measure of energy content. * **Complex Amplitudes:** In advanced topics like AC circuit analysis using phasors or quantum mechanics, "amplitude" can refer to a complex number, where its magnitude is the physical amplitude and its argument (angle) represents the phase. This simplifies calculations for sinusoidal steady-state responses. ### Summary of Key Points * **Definition:** Amplitude is the maximum displacement of a periodic function from its midline. It quantifies the "height" or "strength" of the wave. * **Mathematical Form:** For $y = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$ or $y = A \cos(Bx + C) + D$, the amplitude is $|A|$. * **Always Positive:** Amplitude is always a non-negative value, representing a magnitude. * **Effect of 'A':** * $|A| > 1$: Vertical stretch, taller waves. * $0 < |A| < 1$: Vertical compression, shorter waves. * $A < 0$: Reflection across the midline (in addition to scaling by $|A|$). * **From Graph:** Amplitude = $\frac{\text{Maximum Value} - \text{Minimum Value}}{2}$. * **Real-world Relevance:** Crucial for understanding loudness (sound), brightness (light), peak values (electricity), and the extent of oscillations in various physical systems. * **Advanced Concepts:** Can be variable in damped/modulated oscillations and can be represented by complex numbers in advanced analysis. 8.) Period The concept of "Period" in mathematics is fundamental, particularly when dealing with phenomena that repeat themselves over time or space. It is a cornerstone for understanding oscillating systems, waves, and signals, making it indispensable for engineering disciplines. Let's delve into its definition, properties, applications, and related concepts. --- ### 1. Definition of a Periodic Function At its core, a periodic function is a function that repeats its values in a regular interval or pattern. **Formal Definition:** A function $f: \mathbb{R} \to \mathbb{R}$ is said to be **periodic** if there exists a positive real number $T$ such that for all $x$ in the domain of $f$, the following condition holds: $$f(x + T) = f(x)$$ The smallest positive value of $T$ for which this condition is true is called the **fundamental period** (or simply the period) of the function. If such a smallest positive $T$ does not exist (e.g., for a constant function, any positive $T$ works, so there's no *smallest* one), the function is sometimes referred to as periodic but without a fundamental period, or sometimes simply not considered periodic in the strict sense that implies a unique fundamental period. For non-constant functions, a fundamental period always exists. **Graphical Interpretation:** Graphically, a periodic function looks like a pattern that repeats endlessly along the x-axis. If you take a "slice" of the graph over one period, you can replicate that slice infinitely to reconstruct the entire function. **Analogy:** Imagine a clock. The hour hand completes a full rotation (repeats its position) every 12 hours. So, its motion is periodic with a period of 12 hours. Similarly, the minute hand has a period of 60 minutes. ### 2. Examples of Periodic Functions Periodic functions are ubiquitous in mathematics and science. #### a. Trigonometric Functions These are the most classic examples of periodic functions. * **Sine Function:** $f(x) = \sin(x)$ * Period: $2\pi$ * Proof: We know that $\sin(x + 2\pi) = \sin(x)$ for all $x$. This is the smallest positive value for which the identity holds. * Example: $\sin(0) = 0$, $\sin(2\pi) = 0$, $\sin(4\pi) = 0$. * **Cosine Function:** $f(x) = \cos(x)$ * Period: $2\pi$ * Proof: Similar to sine, $\cos(x + 2\pi) = \cos(x)$. * Example: $\cos(0) = 1$, $\cos(2\pi) = 1$, $\cos(4\pi) = 1$. * **Tangent Function:** $f(x) = \tan(x)$ * Period: $\pi$ * Proof: $\tan(x + \pi) = \frac{\sin(x+\pi)}{\cos(x+\pi)} = \frac{-\sin(x)}{-\cos(x)} = \tan(x)$. This is the smallest positive value. * Example: $\tan(0) = 0$, $\tan(\pi) = 0$, $\tan(2\pi) = 0$. * **Reciprocal Trigonometric Functions:** $\cot(x)$, $\sec(x)$, $\csc(x)$ * $\cot(x)$ has a period of $\pi$. * $\sec(x)$ and $\csc(x)$ have a period of $2\pi$. #### b. Other Common Periodic Waveforms (Common in Engineering) * **Sawtooth Wave:** Defined piecewise, it rises linearly and then drops instantly, repeating this pattern. * Example: $f(x) = x - \lfloor x \rfloor$ (period 1 for $x \ge 0$). More generally, a sawtooth wave can be defined to go from a minimum to a maximum over a period $T$. * **Square Wave:** Alternates regularly between two fixed values (e.g., 1 and -1). * Example: $f(x) = \text{sgn}(\sin(\omega x))$ where $\text{sgn}$ is the sign function. The period would depend on $\omega$. For $\omega=1$, it would be $2\pi$. * **Triangular Wave:** Rises linearly, falls linearly, and then repeats. * Can be derived from the absolute value of the sine function or from integrating a square wave. #### c. Constant Function (Special Case) A constant function, $f(x) = c$, satisfies $f(x+T) = c = f(x)$ for *any* positive real number $T$. Therefore, it is periodic but does not have a *fundamental* period since there is no smallest positive $T$. ### 3. Properties of Periodic Functions Understanding these properties is crucial for manipulating and analyzing periodic signals. #### a. Periodicity of $f(ax+b)$ If $f(x)$ is a periodic function with period $T$, then the function $g(x) = f(ax+b)$ (where $a \ne 0$) is also periodic. **Derivation:** Let $T_g$ be the period of $g(x)$. By definition, $g(x + T_g) = g(x)$. Substituting the definition of $g(x)$: $f(a(x + T_g) + b) = f(ax + b)$ $f(ax + aT_g + b) = f(ax + b)$ Since $f(x)$ has period $T$, we know that $f(Y + T) = f(Y)$ for any $Y$ in its domain. Comparing the arguments of $f$ on both sides, we must have $aT_g = T$. Therefore, $T_g = \frac{T}{|a|}$. We use $|a|$ because a negative $a$ implies a reflection and horizontal scaling, but the period (a length) must be positive. **Example:** If $f(x) = \sin(x)$ has period $2\pi$. Then $g(x) = \sin(2x - \frac{\pi}{4})$ has a period $T_g = \frac{2\pi}{|2|} = \pi$. And $h(x) = \cos(\frac{x}{3})$ has a period $T_h = \frac{2\pi}{|1/3|} = 6\pi$. #### b. Sum/Difference of Periodic Functions If $f(x)$ has period $T_1$ and $g(x)$ has period $T_2$, then their sum $f(x) + g(x)$ (or difference $f(x) - g(x)$) is periodic if the ratio $\frac{T_1}{T_2}$ is a rational number. If $\frac{T_1}{T_2} = \frac{p}{q}$ (a rational number in simplest form), then the period of $f(x) + g(x)$ is $LCM(T_1, T_2)$. More precisely, it is $qT_1 = pT_2$. This is the least common multiple of $T_1$ and $T_2$. **Example:** Let $f(x) = \sin(2x)$ and $g(x) = \cos(3x)$. Period of $f(x)$ is $T_1 = \frac{2\pi}{2} = \pi$. Period of $g(x)$ is $T_2 = \frac{2\pi}{3}$. The ratio $\frac{T_1}{T_2} = \frac{\pi}{2\pi/3} = \frac{3}{2}$, which is rational. The period of $f(x) + g(x)$ will be $LCM(\pi, \frac{2\pi}{3}) = 2\pi \times LCM(1, 2/3)$ This is not how LCM of real numbers works directly. A better way: Find integers $n_1, n_2$ such that $n_1 T_1 = n_2 T_2 = T_{overall}$. $n_1 \pi = n_2 \frac{2\pi}{3}$ $n_1 = \frac{2}{3} n_2$. The smallest integers are $n_1=2, n_2=3$. So, $T_{overall} = 2 \times \pi = 3 \times \frac{2\pi}{3} = 2\pi$. The period of $\sin(2x) + \cos(3x)$ is $2\pi$. **What if the ratio is irrational?** If the ratio $\frac{T_1}{T_2}$ is irrational (e.g., $f(x) = \sin(x)$ and $g(x) = \sin(\sqrt{2}x)$), then $f(x) + g(x)$ is generally **not periodic**. Such functions are sometimes called "almost periodic." This is because their patterns will never precisely align again after the initial point. #### c. Composition of Periodic Functions If $f(x)$ is periodic with period $T_f$, and $g(x)$ is any function such that $g(f(x))$ is defined, then $h(x) = g(f(x))$ is also periodic with period $T_f$ (or a submultiple of $T_f$). This is because if $f(x+T_f) = f(x)$, then $g(f(x+T_f)) = g(f(x))$. **Example:** If $f(x) = \sin(x)$ (period $2\pi$) and $g(y) = y^2$. Then $h(x) = g(f(x)) = (\sin(x))^2 = \sin^2(x)$. We know $\sin^2(x) = \frac{1 - \cos(2x)}{2}$. The period of $\cos(2x)$ is $\frac{2\pi}{2} = \pi$. So, the period of $\sin^2(x)$ is $\pi$. Here, the period ($\pi$) is a submultiple of $2\pi$. This happens when the outer function's operations (like squaring) cause some values to repeat sooner. E.g., $\sin(x) = \sin(x+\pi)$ is false, but $\sin^2(x) = \sin^2(x+\pi)$ is true because $\sin(x+\pi) = -\sin(x)$, and $(-\sin(x))^2 = \sin^2(x)$. #### d. Integration and Differentiation of Periodic Functions * **Differentiation:** If $f(x)$ is differentiable and periodic with period $T$, then $f'(x)$ is also periodic with period $T$. * Proof: $f(x+T) = f(x)$. Differentiating both sides with respect to $x$: $\frac{d}{dx}f(x+T) = \frac{d}{dx}f(x)$ $f'(x+T) = f'(x)$. * Example: If $f(x) = \sin(x)$ (period $2\pi$), then $f'(x) = \cos(x)$ also has period $2\pi$. * **Integration:** If $f(x)$ is periodic with period $T$, then its indefinite integral $F(x) = \int f(x) dx$ is generally *not* periodic unless the integral over one period is zero, i.e., $\int_{a}^{a+T} f(x) dx = 0$. * If $\int_{a}^{a+T} f(x) dx = C \ne 0$, then $F(x+T) = F(x) + C$, so $F(x)$ grows or shrinks linearly over time and is not periodic. * Example: $\int \sin(x) dx = -\cos(x) + C_0$. Since $\sin(x)$ integrates to 0 over $2\pi$, $-\cos(x)$ is periodic with period $2\pi$. * Example where it's not periodic: If $f(x)$ is a square wave that's always positive, say 1 for half a period and 0 for the other half. Its integral will be a ramp function, continuously increasing. ### 4. Derivations and Proofs (Revisited for Specific Cases) #### a. Proving $\sin(x)$ has Period $2\pi$ We rely on the unit circle definition of sine or its Taylor series expansion, but for a 12th-grade level, trigonometric identities are sufficient. We need to show: 1. $\sin(x+2\pi) = \sin(x)$ for all $x$. 2. $2\pi$ is the smallest positive value for which this holds. **Proof of 1:** Using the angle sum identity $\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B$: $\sin(x+2\pi) = \sin x \cos(2\pi) + \cos x \sin(2\pi)$ Since $\cos(2\pi) = 1$ and $\sin(2\pi) = 0$: $\sin(x+2\pi) = \sin x (1) + \cos x (0) = \sin x$. This proves the identity. **Proof of 2 (Smallest Positive Period):** Assume there exists a smaller positive period $T_0 < 2\pi$ such that $\sin(x+T_0) = \sin(x)$ for all $x$. Let $x=0$: $\sin(0+T_0) = \sin(0) \Rightarrow \sin(T_0) = 0$. The positive values of $T_0$ for which $\sin(T_0) = 0$ are $T_0 = \pi, 2\pi, 3\pi, \dots$. So, if $T_0$ is a period, it must be a multiple of $\pi$. Now, check $T_0 = \pi$: Is $\sin(x+\pi) = \sin(x)$ true for all $x$? $\sin(x+\pi) = \sin x \cos\pi + \cos x \sin\pi = \sin x (-1) + \cos x (0) = -\sin x$. Since $-\sin x \ne \sin x$ for all $x$ (e.g., if $x=\pi/2$, $-\sin(\pi/2) = -1$ while $\sin(\pi/2)=1$), $T_0=\pi$ is not a period. Therefore, the smallest positive period must be $2\pi$. #### b. Proving Periodicity for General Linear Transformations Let $f(x)$ be a periodic function with period $T$. Prove that $g(x) = f(ax+b)$ has period $T/|a|$. **Proof:** Let $T_g$ be the period of $g(x)$. By definition, $g(x+T_g) = g(x)$. Substituting $g(x) = f(ax+b)$: $f(a(x+T_g) + b) = f(ax+b)$ $f(ax + aT_g + b) = f(ax+b)$ Since $f$ is periodic with period $T$, this means that $aT_g$ must be a multiple of $T$. For $T_g$ to be the fundamental period, $aT_g$ must be the smallest positive multiple of $T$, which is $T$ itself. So, $aT_g = T$ (assuming $a>0$). This gives $T_g = T/a$. If $a<0$, then $aT_g = T$ would give a negative $T_g$. Since period must be positive, we take the absolute value of $a$. Thus, $T_g = T/|a|$. This derivation is crucial in signal processing and physics, where scaling time or space directly affects the frequency or wavelength of a periodic phenomenon. ### 5. Applications of Periodic Functions in Engineering The concept of periodicity is central to numerous engineering fields. #### a. Signal Processing and Electrical Engineering * **Alternating Current (AC):** The voltage and current in an AC circuit are typically sinusoidal (or sometimes square/triangular waves). The period of the voltage/current determines its frequency (Frequency = 1/Period). E.g., household AC in many countries is 50 Hz or 60 Hz, meaning periods of 0.02s or 0.0167s respectively. * **Sound Waves:** Sound is a pressure wave, and pure tones are represented by sine waves. The period of the wave determines its pitch (frequency). * **Light Waves:** Light, as an electromagnetic wave, also exhibits periodicity. Its period (or wavelength) determines its color. * **Modulation:** In communication systems (AM, FM), information is encoded by varying properties (amplitude, frequency, phase) of a periodic carrier wave. * **Sampling:** Digital signal processing relies on sampling analog periodic signals at discrete intervals, a process governed by the Nyquist-Shannon sampling theorem, which relates sampling rate to the signal's maximum frequency (minimum period). #### b. Mechanical Engineering and Physics * **Simple Harmonic Motion (SHM):** Oscillations of a spring-mass system, a simple pendulum (for small angles), or a vibrating string are all classic examples of SHM, which is described by sinusoidal functions. The period of oscillation is a key characteristic. * For a spring-mass system: $T = 2\pi\sqrt{m/k}$ (where $m$ is mass, $k$ is spring constant). * For a simple pendulum: $T = 2\pi\sqrt{L/g}$ (where $L$ is length, $g$ is gravity). * **Waves in Media:** Water waves, seismic waves, and waves on a string are all periodic phenomena characterized by their period, wavelength, and speed. * **Rotational Motion:** The motion of a rotating machine part (e.g., a crankshaft) is periodic. #### c. Fourier Series and Analysis One of the most powerful applications of periodic functions is the **Fourier Series**. It states that any "well-behaved" periodic function (with period $T$) can be represented as a sum of sines and cosines with periods $T, T/2, T/3, \ldots$ (i.e., fundamental frequency and its harmonics). **Formula:** For a periodic function $f(x)$ with period $T = 2L$, the Fourier series is: $$f(x) = a_0 + \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( a_n \cos\left(\frac{n\pi x}{L}\right) + b_n \sin\left(\frac{n\pi x}{L}\right) \right)$$ where the coefficients are given by: $$a_0 = \frac{1}{2L} \int_{-L}^{L} f(x) dx$$ $$a_n = \frac{1}{L} \int_{-L}^{L} f(x) \cos\left(\frac{n\pi x}{L}\right) dx$$ $$b_n = \frac{1}{L} \int_{-L}^{L} f(x) \sin\left(\frac{n\pi x}{L}\right) dx$$ **Importance for Engineering:** * **Signal Decomposition:** Allows engineers to break down complex, non-sinusoidal periodic signals (like square waves or human speech) into their constituent pure sine and cosine waves. This is vital for analyzing signal content, filtering noise, and designing circuits. * **System Response:** If a linear system (e.g., an RC circuit) is subjected to a periodic input, the output can be found by analyzing how the system responds to each individual sinusoidal component in the Fourier series. * **Data Compression:** In image and audio compression (e.g., JPEG, MP3), Fourier-related transforms (like Discrete Cosine Transform) are used to represent data efficiently by storing only the most significant frequency components. ### 6. Related Concepts & Interesting Points #### a. Anti-periodicity A function $f(x)$ is **anti-periodic** with anti-period $T$ if $f(x+T) = -f(x)$. Example: $\sin(x)$ is anti-periodic with anti-period $\pi$. $\sin(x+\pi) = -\sin(x)$. Note that an anti-periodic function with anti-period $T$ is also a periodic function with period $2T$. $f(x+2T) = f((x+T)+T) = -f(x+T) = -(-f(x)) = f(x)$. #### b. Almost Periodic Functions These are functions that are not strictly periodic but exhibit a repeating behavior that can be approximated arbitrarily well by sums of periodic functions. Their "periods" are incommensurable (their ratio is irrational). Example: $\sin(x) + \sin(\sqrt{2}x)$. While not strictly periodic, its behavior is "almost" repetitive. These functions are important in areas like fluid dynamics and celestial mechanics. #### c. Discrete Periodicity (Sequences) The concept of periodicity also applies to sequences. A sequence $\{a_n\}$ is periodic if there exists a positive integer $P$ (the period) such that $a_{n+P} = a_n$ for all $n$. Example: The sequence $1, 0, -1, 0, 1, 0, -1, 0, \ldots$ has a period of 4. This is crucial in digital signal processing for discrete-time signals and sequences. #### d. Periodicity in Modular Arithmetic In number theory, periodicity emerges in modular arithmetic. Example: The last digit of powers of 2: $2^1=2, 2^2=4, 2^3=8, 2^4=16 \to 6, 2^5=32 \to 2, \ldots$. The sequence of last digits $2, 4, 8, 6, 2, 4, 8, 6, \ldots$ is periodic with period 4. This is related to Euler's totient theorem and Fermat's Little Theorem. #### e. Periodicity in Chaos Theory (Periodic Orbits) In the study of dynamical systems and chaos theory, a **periodic orbit** (or cycle) refers to a trajectory or sequence of states in a system that repeats itself exactly after a fixed number of iterations or a fixed time interval. While chaotic systems are often characterized by sensitive dependence on initial conditions and non-repeating long-term behavior, they can also contain embedded periodic orbits, which are crucial for understanding the system's underlying structure. The stability of these orbits is a significant area of study. --- ### Summary of Key Points * **Definition:** A function $f(x)$ is periodic if $f(x+T) = f(x)$ for some positive $T$. The smallest such $T$ is the **fundamental period**. * **Common Examples:** Trigonometric functions ($\sin(x)$, $\cos(x)$ with period $2\pi$; $\tan(x)$ with period $\pi$), square waves, sawtooth waves. * **Transformations:** If $f(x)$ has period $T$, then $f(ax+b)$ has period $T/|a|$. * **Combinations:** The sum/difference of periodic functions is periodic if the ratio of their periods is rational, with the new period being the LCM of the individual periods. Otherwise, it's often almost periodic. * **Calculus:** The derivative of a periodic function is periodic with the same period. The integral is generally not periodic unless the integral over one period is zero. * **Engineering Significance:** Periodicity is central to signal processing (AC circuits, sound, light), mechanical vibrations (SHM), and the powerful **Fourier Series** for decomposing complex signals into simple sinusoidal components. * **Broader Context:** Periodicity extends to sequences (discrete signals), modular arithmetic, and even appears as "periodic orbits" in complex dynamical systems. Understanding "period" provides a powerful lens through which to analyze and design systems in diverse engineering fields, from electrical circuits to mechanical vibrations and beyond. 9.) Phase Shift ## Phase Shift: A Comprehensive Exploration for Engineering Students ### 1. Introduction to Periodic Functions To understand phase shift, we must first grasp the concept of periodic functions. * **Definition:** A function $f(x)$ is **periodic** if there exists a positive constant $P$ such that $f(x + P) = f(x)$ for all $x$ in the domain of $f$. The smallest such positive constant $P$ is called the **period** of the function. * *Analogy:* Imagine a recurring pattern on a wallpaper. The period is the smallest length after which the pattern repeats itself. * **Examples:** The most common periodic functions in engineering are sinusoidal functions: sine ($y = \sin(x)$) and cosine ($y = \cos(x)$). * Both $\sin(x)$ and $\cos(x)$ have a period of $2\pi$ radians (or $360^\circ$). * **Key Properties of Sinusoidal Functions:** * **Amplitude (A):** The maximum displacement or distance moved by a point on a vibrating body or wave measured from its equilibrium position. For $y = A \sin(x)$, the amplitude is $|A|$. * **Period (T):** The time or spatial interval over which the function's values repeat. For $y = \sin(Bx)$, the period is $T = \frac{2\pi}{|B|}$. * **Frequency (f):** The number of complete cycles occurring per unit of time or space. It is the reciprocal of the period: $f = \frac{1}{T}$. Angular frequency $\omega = 2\pi f = |B|$. ### 2. What is Phase? Before defining phase shift, let's understand "phase" itself. * **Definition:** The **phase** of a periodic function (like a wave) at a specific point in time or space refers to the position or stage of the cycle at that point. It's essentially the current "progress" through one complete cycle. * *Analogy:* Consider a clock with only an hour hand. The "phase" of the hour hand indicates how far along it is in its 12-hour cycle (e.g., 3 o'clock, 6 o'clock). For a race car on a circular track, its phase indicates its position around the track in its current lap. * **Units of Phase:** Phase is typically measured in angles (radians or degrees) or as a fraction of a cycle. A full cycle corresponds to $2\pi$ radians or $360^\circ$. ### 3. What is Phase Shift? Now we can define the core concept. * **Definition:** **Phase shift** (or phase difference) is the horizontal translation or displacement of a periodic function from its usual position. It describes how much one wave is "ahead" or "behind" another wave, or how much a single wave is shifted from a reference point (e.g., the standard $\sin(x)$ or $\cos(x)$ function). * *Visual Representation:* If you graph $y = \sin(x)$ and $y = \sin(x - \phi)$, the second graph is the first graph shifted horizontally by $\phi$ units. * **Positive vs. Negative Phase Shift:** * A **positive phase shift** (e.g., $\sin(x - \phi)$ where $\phi > 0$) means the function is shifted to the **right**. This is often referred to as **lagging** because the shifted wave reaches its peak/zero crossing later than the reference wave. * A **negative phase shift** (e.g., $\sin(x + \phi)$ where $\phi > 0$) means the function is shifted to the **left**. This is often referred to as **leading** because the shifted wave reaches its peak/zero crossing earlier than the reference wave. * *Convention Note:* Be careful with conventions! In electrical engineering, current "leading" voltage implies current reaches its peak earlier than voltage. If voltage is $V = V_0 \sin(\omega t)$ and current is $I = I_0 \sin(\omega t + \phi)$, then $\phi$ positive means current *leads* voltage. This corresponds to a left shift of the current waveform. ### 4. Mathematical Representation of Phase Shift Let's look at the general form of a sinusoidal function and identify the phase shift. * **General Form:** A general sinusoidal function can be written as: $y(x) = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$ or, alternatively and more intuitively for phase shift: $y(x) = A \sin(B(x - \frac{-C}{B})) + D$ or even: $y(x) = A \sin(B(x - \phi)) + D$ Where: * **A:** Amplitude. * **B:** Determines the angular frequency ($\omega = |B|$) and period ($T = \frac{2\pi}{|B|}$). * **C:** The **phase constant** (sometimes called the phase shift itself, but this can be misleading as the actual horizontal shift depends on B). * **D:** Vertical shift (midline of the wave is $y=D$). * **$\phi$:** The actual **phase shift**. * **Derivation of Phase Shift (Horizontal Shift):** To isolate the horizontal shift, we need to factor out $B$ from the argument of the sine function: $y(x) = A \sin(B(x + \frac{C}{B})) + D$ Comparing this to the standard form of a horizontally shifted function $f(x - h)$, where $h$ is the shift: $x - h = x + \frac{C}{B}$ So, $h = -\frac{C}{B}$. Thus, the **phase shift** is $\phi = -\frac{C}{B}$. * If $\phi > 0$ (meaning $C$ is negative), the graph shifts to the right (lags). * If $\phi < 0$ (meaning $C$ is positive), the graph shifts to the left (leads). * **Units:** The phase shift $\phi$ will have the same units as $x$ (e.g., seconds for time-varying functions $y(t)$, or meters for spatially varying functions $y(x)$). The phase constant $C$ will have units of radians or degrees. ### 5. Detailed Analysis of Parameters Let's revisit the parameters in $y(x) = A \sin(B(x - \phi)) + D$: * **Amplitude (A):** The peak deviation from the center line. It determines the height of the wave. If $A$ is negative, it indicates a vertical reflection across the midline. * Range: $[D-|A|, D+|A|]$. * **Angular Frequency (B) or $\omega$:** This parameter dictates how rapidly the wave oscillates. * **Period (T):** $T = \frac{2\pi}{|B|}$. This is the length of one complete cycle. * **Frequency (f):** $f = \frac{1}{T} = \frac{|B|}{2\pi}$. This is the number of cycles per unit of $x$. * **Angular Frequency ($\omega$):** $\omega = |B|$. This is the rate of change of phase, measured in radians per unit of $x$. For time-varying signals, $\omega$ is in rad/s. * *Derivation of Period:* Consider $y = \sin(Bx)$. For one cycle, the argument of the sine function must go from $0$ to $2\pi$. So, $B x_{cycle} = 2\pi \Rightarrow x_{cycle} = \frac{2\pi}{B}$. This $x_{cycle}$ is the period $T$. * **Phase Shift ($\phi$):** The horizontal translation. It determines the starting point of the wave's cycle relative to $x=0$. * $\phi = -C/B$. * If $\phi > 0$, shift right (lagging). * If $\phi < 0$, shift left (leading). * **Vertical Shift (D):** The vertical translation of the entire wave. It represents the midline (equilibrium position) around which the wave oscillates. * If $D > 0$, shift upwards. * If $D < 0$, shift downwards. ### 6. Examples and Applications #### Example 1: Basic Phase Shift Calculation **Problem:** Identify the amplitude, period, and phase shift of the function $y = 3 \sin(2x + \frac{\pi}{4}) - 1$. **Solution:** The function is in the form $y = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$. Comparing: $A=3$, $B=2$, $C=\frac{\pi}{4}$, $D=-1$. 1. **Amplitude (A):** $|A| = |3| = 3$. 2. **Period (T):** $T = \frac{2\pi}{|B|} = \frac{2\pi}{2} = \pi$. 3. **Phase Shift ($\phi$):** $\phi = -\frac{C}{B} = -\frac{\pi/4}{2} = -\frac{\pi}{8}$. Since $\phi = -\frac{\pi}{8}$ (a negative value), the wave is shifted $\frac{\pi}{8}$ units to the **left** (leading). 4. **Vertical Shift (D):** $D = -1$. The midline is $y=-1$. #### Example 2: Graphing with Phase Shift **Problem:** Sketch one cycle of $y = 2 \cos(x - \frac{\pi}{2})$. **Solution:** Here, $A=2$, $B=1$, $\phi = \frac{\pi}{2}$ (since it's in $B(x-\phi)$ form, so $C = -B\phi = -\pi/2$), $D=0$. 1. **Amplitude:** 2. 2. **Period:** $T = \frac{2\pi}{1} = 2\pi$. 3. **Phase Shift:** $\phi = \frac{\pi}{2}$. Shifted $\frac{\pi}{2}$ units to the right (lagging). 4. **Midline:** $y=0$. * The standard $\cos(x)$ starts at its maximum at $x=0$. * Due to the phase shift of $\frac{\pi}{2}$ to the right, $y = 2 \cos(x - \frac{\pi}{2})$ will start its cycle (reach its maximum) at $x = \frac{\pi}{2}$. * The cycle will complete at $x = \frac{\pi}{2} + 2\pi = \frac{5\pi}{2}$. * The maximum value is $2$, minimum is $-2$. *Steps to sketch:* 1. Draw the midline $y=0$. 2. Identify starting point of the shifted cycle: For cosine, it's typically a peak. Standard $\cos(x)$ peaks at $x=0$. Here, $x - \frac{\pi}{2} = 0 \Rightarrow x = \frac{\pi}{2}$. So, $( \frac{\pi}{2}, 2 )$ is a peak. 3. Identify end point of the cycle: $x_{end} = \frac{\pi}{2} + 2\pi = \frac{5\pi}{2}$. So, $( \frac{5\pi}{2}, 2 )$ is another peak. 4. Midpoints for zero crossings and minimums: * Zero crossing: $x = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{T}{4} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{2\pi}{4} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{\pi}{2} = \pi$. So $(\pi, 0)$. * Minimum: $x = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{T}{2} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \pi = \frac{3\pi}{2}$. So $(\frac{3\pi}{2}, -2)$. * Zero crossing: $x = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{3T}{4} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{3(2\pi)}{4} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{3\pi}{2} = 2\pi$. So $(2\pi, 0)$. This function is actually equivalent to $2\sin(x)$! **Proof:** We know from trigonometric identities that $\cos(\theta - \frac{\pi}{2}) = \sin(\theta)$. Let $\theta = x$. Then $2 \cos(x - \frac{\pi}{2}) = 2 \sin(x)$. This shows that $\sin(x)$ is simply $\cos(x)$ shifted $\frac{\pi}{2}$ to the right. Or $\cos(x)$ is $\sin(x)$ shifted $\frac{\pi}{2}$ to the left. $\cos(x) = \sin(x + \frac{\pi}{2})$. #### Example 3: Constructing Equation from Graph **Problem:** A sinusoidal wave has a peak at $(1, 5)$ and the next trough (minimum) at $(3, -1)$. The midline is between the peak and trough. Find its equation in the form $y = A \cos(B(x - \phi)) + D$. **Solution:** 1. **Midline (D):** The midline is halfway between the peak and trough y-values. $D = \frac{5 + (-1)}{2} = \frac{4}{2} = 2$. 2. **Amplitude (A):** The amplitude is the distance from the midline to a peak (or trough). $A = 5 - D = 5 - 2 = 3$. (Or $D - (-1) = 2 - (-1) = 3$). 3. **Period (T):** The distance from a peak to the next trough is half a period. $\frac{T}{2} = 3 - 1 = 2$. So, $T = 4$. 4. **Parameter B:** $T = \frac{2\pi}{|B|} \Rightarrow 4 = \frac{2\pi}{B} \Rightarrow B = \frac{2\pi}{4} = \frac{\pi}{2}$. 5. **Phase Shift ($\phi$):** We are using cosine, which naturally starts at a peak. The first peak given is at $x=1$. So, the horizontal shift for the cosine function is $\phi = 1$. Therefore, the equation is $y = 3 \cos(\frac{\pi}{2}(x - 1)) + 2$. #### Real-world Applications of Phase Shift: * **Electrical Engineering (AC Circuits):** * **Voltage and Current Relationship:** In AC circuits with capacitors or inductors, the current and voltage waveforms are often out of phase. * In a purely inductive circuit, current lags voltage by $90^\circ$ ($\pi/2$ radians). * In a purely capacitive circuit, current leads voltage by $90^\circ$ ($\pi/2$ radians). * **Power Factor:** The phase difference between voltage and current determines the power factor, which indicates how effectively electrical power is being used. A phase shift leads to reactive power, which does no useful work. * **Impedance:** Complex impedance, $Z = R + jX$, naturally represents resistance and reactance, with the phase angle $\theta = \arctan(X/R)$ representing the phase shift between voltage and current. * **Mechanical Vibrations:** * When an external force acts on an oscillating system (e.g., a mass-spring system), the response (displacement) can be out of phase with the driving force, especially near resonance. Damping also introduces phase shifts. * **Optics (Wave Interference):** * When light waves combine, their phase difference determines whether they interfere constructively (in phase, increasing amplitude) or destructively (180$^\circ$ out of phase, canceling out). This is fundamental to phenomena like thin-film interference and diffraction. * **Sound Waves:** * Similar to light, sound waves can interfere. Noise-canceling headphones work by generating a sound wave that is 180$^\circ$ out of phase with the unwanted ambient noise, causing destructive interference. * **Signal Processing:** * **Filters:** Electronic filters can introduce phase shifts that vary with frequency, which is crucial in audio processing and communication systems. * **Modulation:** Techniques like Phase-Shift Keying (PSK) encode digital data by varying the phase of a carrier wave. ### 7. Leading vs. Lagging Phase Shift Reiterating the convention for clarity: * Let a reference wave be $y_1(t) = A \sin(\omega t)$. * Let a second wave be $y_2(t) = A \sin(\omega t + \phi_{ps})$. * If $\phi_{ps} > 0$: $y_2(t)$ is shifted to the **left**. This means $y_2(t)$ reaches its peaks/zero crossings **earlier** than $y_1(t)$. We say $y_2(t)$ **leads** $y_1(t)$. * *Example:* $\sin(t + \pi/2) = \cos(t)$. Cosine leads sine by $\pi/2$. * If $\phi_{ps} < 0$: $y_2(t)$ is shifted to the **right**. This means $y_2(t)$ reaches its peaks/zero crossings **later** than $y_1(t)$. We say $y_2(t)$ **lags** $y_1(t)$. * *Example:* $\sin(t - \pi/2) = -\cos(t)$. The negative cosine lags sine by $\pi/2$. * **Phase Difference in Degrees vs. Time:** Phase difference $\Delta \theta$ (in radians or degrees) is related to the time difference $\Delta t$ by: $\Delta t = \frac{\Delta \theta}{\omega} = \frac{\Delta \theta}{2\pi f} = \frac{\Delta \theta \cdot T}{2\pi}$. Where $\omega$ is angular frequency and $T$ is period. So, a phase shift of $\pi$ radians corresponds to a time shift of $T/2$. ### 8. Periodicity and Phase Shift Equivalence Because periodic functions repeat, a phase shift can sometimes be equivalent to a different phase shift or even no shift at all. * **Equivalence:** Adding or subtracting integer multiples of the period does not change the function. For example, $\sin(x)$ is the same as $\sin(x + 2\pi)$, $\sin(x + 4\pi)$, or $\sin(x - 2\pi)$. This means a phase shift of $\phi$ is equivalent to a phase shift of $\phi \pm 2\pi n$ for any integer $n$. Often, the phase shift is conventionally expressed as an angle between $-\pi$ and $\pi$ (or $-180^\circ$ and $180^\circ$). ### 9. Measurement of Phase Shift * **Oscilloscope:** In electronics, an oscilloscope is commonly used to visualize two waveforms simultaneously and measure the time difference between their corresponding points (e.g., zero crossings or peaks). From this time difference and the period, the phase shift can be calculated using the formula from Section 7: $\Delta \theta = \frac{\Delta t}{T} \cdot 2\pi$. * **Lissajous Figures:** When two sinusoidal signals of the same frequency but different phases are applied to the horizontal and vertical inputs of an oscilloscope in X-Y mode, they produce characteristic patterns called Lissajous figures. The shape of the figure (e.g., an ellipse, circle, or line) reveals the phase difference. * **Mathematical Methods:** * **Cross-correlation:** This statistical method measures the similarity between two signals as a function of the time lag applied to one of them. The time lag that maximizes the cross-correlation corresponds to the phase shift. * **Fourier Analysis:** By transforming signals into the frequency domain (e.g., using a Fast Fourier Transform - FFT), the phase of each frequency component can be directly obtained. This is particularly useful for complex signals made of multiple frequencies. ### 10. Interesting Points / Advanced Concepts * **Phase Difference vs. Phase Shift:** While often used interchangeably, "phase difference" generally refers to the angular difference between two waves at a specific point in time/space, whereas "phase shift" specifically implies the horizontal translation of one wave relative to a reference. However, the underlying mathematical concept is the same. * **Phase Velocity vs. Group Velocity:** (Briefly) * **Phase Velocity:** The speed at which a point of constant phase (e.g., a wave crest) propagates through space. $v_p = \frac{\omega}{k}$, where $k$ is the wave number ($k=B$). * **Group Velocity:** The speed at which the overall envelope of a wave (e.g., a pulse formed by multiple frequencies) propagates. This is often the speed at which energy or information travels. In non-dispersive media, phase and group velocities are the same. In dispersive media, they differ, which is crucial in fiber optics. * **Phase Locked Loops (PLLs):** These are electronic circuits widely used in communication systems (e.g., FM demodulation, frequency synthesis) to synchronize the phase of an oscillator with the phase of an input signal. They track the phase of an incoming signal, which is critical for robust data recovery. * **Hilbert Transform:** In signal processing, the Hilbert transform can be used to derive the instantaneous phase of a real-valued signal, separating it into its amplitude and phase components. This is vital in analytical signal representation. ### Summary of Key Points: * **Periodic functions** repeat their values over a fixed interval (period). * **Phase** indicates the position within a cycle of a periodic function. * **Phase shift** is the horizontal translation of a periodic function. * For $y = A \sin(B(x - \phi)) + D$, the phase shift is $\phi$. For $y = A \sin(Bx + C) + D$, the phase shift is $\phi = -\frac{C}{B}$. * A **positive $\phi$** (or negative $C$) means a shift to the **right** (lagging). * A **negative $\phi$** (or positive $C$) means a shift to the **left** (leading). * Phase shift is a critical concept in **electrical engineering** (AC circuits, power factor), **wave phenomena** (optics, sound, interference), and **signal processing**. * It can be measured using **oscilloscopes** or determined through **mathematical analysis** like Fourier transforms or cross-correlation. * Because of periodicity, phase shifts differing by integer multiples of the period are equivalent. 10.) Vertical Shift) ### Vertical Shift Vertical shift, also known as vertical translation, is a fundamental concept in the study of function transformations in mathematics. It describes the upward or downward movement of a function's graph along the y-axis, without altering its shape, orientation, or size. This rigid transformation is crucial for understanding how changes in a function's equation relate to changes in its graphical representation, a skill invaluable for engineering students dealing with data, signals, and system modeling. --- #### 1. Definition and Basic Concept * **Definition:** A vertical shift occurs when a constant value is added to or subtracted from the output (y-value) of every point on a function's graph. If a function is defined as $y = f(x)$, a vertical shift transforms it into $y = f(x) + k$, where $k$ is a real constant. * **Intuition:** Imagine picking up the entire graph of a function and moving it directly upwards or downwards. Every point on the graph moves the same distance in the same vertical direction. * **The Role of 'k':** * If $k > 0$ (a positive value), the graph shifts **upwards** by $|k|$ units. * If $k < 0$ (a negative value), the graph shifts **downwards** by $|k|$ units. * If $k = 0$, there is no vertical shift; the function remains unchanged. * **Example 1.1 (Intuitive Shift):** Consider the simplest function, $f(x) = x$. Its graph is a straight line passing through the origin with a slope of 1. * If we apply a vertical shift with $k = 3$, the new function is $g(x) = f(x) + 3 = x + 3$. The line moves 3 units upwards. * Original point (0,0) moves to (0,3). * Original point (1,1) moves to (1,4). * If we apply a vertical shift with $k = -2$, the new function is $h(x) = f(x) - 2 = x - 2$. The line moves 2 units downwards. * Original point (0,0) moves to (0,-2). * Original point (1,1) moves to (1,-1). --- #### 2. Mathematical Representation and Derivation Let's formalize how the coordinates of points on the graph are affected. * **Original Function:** Let $y = f(x)$ be our original function. This means that for any input $x$, the corresponding output is $y = f(x)$. A point on this graph can be represented as $(x, y)$. * **Transformed Function:** When we apply a vertical shift by a constant $k$, the new function is $g(x) = f(x) + k$. For any input $x$, the new output is $y' = f(x) + k$. * **Derivation of Coordinate Transformation:** Consider an arbitrary point $(x_0, y_0)$ on the graph of $f(x)$. By definition of $f(x)$, we have $y_0 = f(x_0)$. Now, consider the new function $g(x) = f(x) + k$. For the same input $x_0$, the output of $g(x)$ will be $g(x_0) = f(x_0) + k$. Since $f(x_0) = y_0$, we can substitute to get $g(x_0) = y_0 + k$. Thus, the point $(x_0, y_0)$ on $f(x)$ is transformed to $(x_0, y_0 + k)$ on $g(x)$. This confirms that the x-coordinate remains unchanged, while the y-coordinate is simply increased by $k$ (or decreased if $k$ is negative). * **Proof (Rigid Transformation):** A rigid transformation preserves distances between points. Let $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ be two points on $f(x)$. The distance between them is $D = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$. After a vertical shift, these points become $(x_1, y_1+k)$ and $(x_2, y_2+k)$. The new distance $D' = \sqrt{((x_2)-(x_1))^2 + ((y_2+k)-(y_1+k))^2}$ $D' = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2+k-y_1-k)^2}$ $D' = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2} = D$. Since $D' = D$, the distance between any two points is preserved, confirming that a vertical shift is a rigid transformation that maintains the shape and size of the graph. --- #### 3. Impact on Key Features of a Function A vertical shift selectively impacts different characteristics of a function. * **Domain:** **Unaffected.** The set of all possible input values ($x$) remains the same because the transformation only changes the output ($y$) for each $x$, not the $x$ values themselves. * *Example:* If $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$, its domain is $[0, \infty)$. If $g(x) = \sqrt{x} + 5$, its domain is still $[0, \infty)$. * **Range:** **Affected.** The set of all possible output values ($y$) shifts by $k$ units. If the original range was $[y_{min}, y_{max}]$, the new range becomes $[y_{min}+k, y_{max}+k]$. * *Derivation:* If $y \in [y_{min}, y_{max}]$, then $y_{min} \le y \le y_{max}$. Adding $k$ to all parts of the inequality gives $y_{min}+k \le y+k \le y_{max}+k$. Since the new output is $y+k$, the new range is $[y_{min}+k, y_{max}+k]$. * *Example:* For $f(x) = x^2$, the range is $[0, \infty)$. For $g(x) = x^2 - 3$, the range becomes $[-3, \infty)$. * **Intercepts:** * **Y-intercept:** **Affected.** If the original y-intercept is $(0, f(0))$, the new y-intercept is $(0, f(0)+k)$. * **X-intercepts (Roots):** **Potentially affected or changed.** The x-intercepts are points where $f(x) = 0$. After a vertical shift, we look for $f(x) + k = 0$, or $f(x) = -k$. This means the new x-intercepts are found where the original function crosses the horizontal line $y = -k$. This can introduce new x-intercepts, eliminate existing ones, or shift their positions. * *Example:* For $f(x) = x^2 - 1$, x-intercepts are $(-1,0)$ and $(1,0)$. * If $g(x) = (x^2 - 1) + 2 = x^2 + 1$, there are no x-intercepts (the graph shifts up, above the x-axis). * If $h(x) = (x^2 - 1) - 3 = x^2 - 4$, new x-intercepts are $(-2,0)$ and $(2,0)$ (the graph shifts down, causing new intersections). * **Asymptotes:** * **Vertical Asymptotes:** **Unaffected.** These are vertical lines determined by specific $x$-values where the function approaches infinity. Since $x$-values are not changed by a vertical shift, vertical asymptotes remain in place. * **Horizontal Asymptotes:** **Affected.** If $f(x)$ has a horizontal asymptote at $y = L$, then $f(x)+k$ will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = L+k$. * **Oblique/Slant Asymptotes:** **Affected.** Similarly, if $f(x)$ has an oblique asymptote $y = mx + b$, then $f(x)+k$ will have an oblique asymptote $y = mx + b + k$. * *Example:* For $f(x) = 1/x$, the horizontal asymptote is $y=0$ and the vertical asymptote is $x=0$. * For $g(x) = 1/x + 5$, the horizontal asymptote shifts to $y=5$, while the vertical asymptote remains $x=0$. * **Extrema (Local Maxima/Minima):** **Affected (values), Unaffected (locations).** The $y$-values of local maxima and minima change by $k$ units, but the $x$-values where these extrema occur remain the same. The shape of the peaks and valleys does not change. * *Example:* For $f(x) = x^2 - 2x + 1 = (x-1)^2$, there's a local minimum at $(1,0)$. * For $g(x) = (x-1)^2 + 4$, the local minimum is at $(1,4)$. * **Inflection Points:** **Affected (values), Unaffected (locations).** Similar to extrema, the $y$-values of inflection points shift by $k$, while their $x$-coordinates stay the same. The concavity of the function is not altered. * **Symmetry:** * **Even Functions:** If $f(x)$ is even ($f(-x) = f(x)$), then $f(x)+k$ is even if and only if $k=0$. If $k \ne 0$, the function $g(x) = f(x)+k$ is no longer even unless the definition of evenness is adjusted to $g(-x) - k = g(x) - k$. However, typically, $g(-x)=g(x)$ is the definition. For example, $f(x) = x^2$ is even. $g(x) = x^2+1$ is also even. This is an exception: if the axis of symmetry is the y-axis, a vertical shift maintains that symmetry. * **Odd Functions:** If $f(x)$ is odd ($f(-x) = -f(x)$), then $f(x)+k$ is no longer odd unless $k=0$. This is because $g(-x) = f(-x)+k = -f(x)+k$. For $g(x)$ to be odd, $g(-x) = -g(x)$, so $-f(x)+k = -(f(x)+k) = -f(x)-k$, which implies $k = -k$, thus $k=0$. * *Conclusion:* A vertical shift generally breaks odd symmetry unless $k=0$. It preserves even symmetry *if* the function's axis of symmetry is the y-axis (i.e., it's of the form $f(x) = f(-x)$). --- #### 4. Examples with Different Function Types Let's illustrate vertical shifts with various common functions. * **Linear Functions:** $f(x) = mx + c$ * Shifted: $g(x) = (mx + c) + k = mx + (c+k)$ * *Observation:* The slope ($m$) remains unchanged. The y-intercept ($c$) simply shifts to $c+k$. * *Example 4.1:* $f(x) = 2x - 1$ * Y-intercept: $(0,-1)$. * Shift up by 3: $g(x) = (2x - 1) + 3 = 2x + 2$. * New Y-intercept: $(0,2)$. The line moves up, parallel to the original. * **Quadratic Functions:** $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ * Shifted: $g(x) = (ax^2 + bx + c) + k$ * *Observation:* The parabola opens in the same direction, and its width remains unchanged. The vertex $(h,v)$ of $f(x)$ shifts to $(h, v+k)$. The axis of symmetry ($x=h$) is unaffected. * *Example 4.2:* $f(x) = x^2$ (vertex at (0,0)) * Shift down by 4: $g(x) = x^2 - 4$. * New vertex: $(0,-4)$. * Original roots: $x=0$. New roots: $x^2-4=0 \Rightarrow x=\pm 2$. * **Cubic Functions:** $f(x) = ax^3 + bx^2 + cx + d$ * Shifted: $g(x) = (ax^3 + bx^2 + cx + d) + k$ * *Observation:* The general shape (e.g., 'S' curve) is preserved. The point of inflection (if any) shifts vertically. * *Example 4.3:* $f(x) = x^3 - 3x$ (inflection point at (0,0)) * Shift up by 2: $g(x) = x^3 - 3x + 2$. * New inflection point: $(0,2)$. * **Trigonometric Functions:** $f(x) = A \sin(Bx + C) + D_{original}$ * Shifted: $g(x) = A \sin(Bx + C) + D_{original} + k$ * *Observation:* The amplitude ($A$), period ($2\pi/B$), and phase shift ($C$) are unaffected. The "midline" or vertical shift ($D_{original}$) changes. This is the DC offset in signal processing. * *Example 4.4:* $f(x) = \sin(x)$ (midline at $y=0$, range $[-1,1]$) * Shift up by 1: $g(x) = \sin(x) + 1$. * New midline: $y=1$. New range: $[0,2]$. * **Exponential Functions:** $f(x) = a^x$ or $f(x) = Ae^{Bx}$ * Shifted: $g(x) = a^x + k$ or $g(x) = Ae^{Bx} + k$ * *Observation:* The horizontal asymptote changes. For $f(x)=a^x$, the horizontal asymptote is $y=0$. For $g(x)=a^x+k$, it becomes $y=k$. * *Example 4.5:* $f(x) = e^x$ (HA at $y=0$) * Shift down by 2: $g(x) = e^x - 2$. * New HA: $y=-2$. * **Logarithmic Functions:** $f(x) = \log_b(x)$ * Shifted: $g(x) = \log_b(x) + k$ * *Observation:* The vertical asymptote (for $f(x)=\log_b(x)$ it's $x=0$) is unaffected, as only the y-values change. The domain remains the same. * *Example 4.6:* $f(x) = \ln(x)$ (VA at $x=0$, domain $(0, \infty)$) * Shift up by 3: $g(x) = \ln(x) + 3$. * VA: $x=0$. Domain: $(0, \infty)$. * **Rational Functions:** $f(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}$ * Shifted: $g(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)} + k$ * *Observation:* Vertical asymptotes are unaffected. Horizontal or oblique asymptotes shift vertically by $k$. * *Example 4.7:* $f(x) = \frac{1}{x-2}$ (VA at $x=2$, HA at $y=0$) * Shift down by 1: $g(x) = \frac{1}{x-2} - 1$. * New HA: $y=-1$. VA: $x=2$. --- #### 5. Combining with Other Transformations When multiple transformations are applied to a function, the order of operations matters, especially with scaling (stretching/compressing) and shifting.
