If $S$ is non-empty (which it is, e.g., choose $k$ such that $bk \le a$), by the Well-Ordering Principle, $S$ has a smallest element, say $r_0 = a - bq_0$. We then show $0 \le r_0 < |b|$. If $r_0 \ge |b|$, then $r_0 - |b| \ge 0$, and $a - bq_0 - |b|$ (or $a - b(q_0 \pm 1)$) would be in $S$ and smaller than $r_0$, a contradiction. * **Example:** For $a = 17, b = 5$: $17 = 5 \times 3 + 2$. Here, $q = 3$ (quotient) and $r = 2$ (remainder). For $a = -17, b = 5$: $-17 = 5 \times (-4) + 3$. Here, $q = -4$ and $r = 3$ (note $0 \le 3 < 5$). ### 3. Types of Quotients The meaning of "quotient" can vary depending on the number system or context. * **Integer Quotient:** The result of integer division, where any fractional part is discarded. This often corresponds to the `floor` function ($\lfloor a/b \rfloor$) for positive divisors, or `truncation` (towards zero) in programming languages. * Example: $17 \div 5 = 3$. In Python, `17 // 5` gives `3`. * Example: $-17 \div 5 = -4$ (floor). In Python, `-17 // 5` gives `-4`. * Example: $-17 \div 5 = -3$ (truncation). In C++/Java, `-17 / 5` gives `-3`. * **Rational Quotient (Fractions):** Expressed as a fraction $p/q$, where $p, q$ are integers and $q \ne 0$. This represents an exact value. * Example: $3/4$, which is $0.75$. * **Real Quotient (Decimal Representation):** The result of division of real numbers, which may be a terminating or non-terminating decimal. * Example: $10/3 = 3.333...$ * Example: $\pi/2 \approx 1.570796...$ * **Complex Quotient:** Division of complex numbers $z_1 = a+bi$ by $z_2 = c+di$ ($z_2 \ne 0$). * **Derivation:** We multiply the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator: $\frac{a+bi}{c+di} = \frac{(a+bi)(c-di)}{(c+di)(c-di)} = \frac{ac - adi + bci - b(i^2)}{c^2 - (di)^2} = \frac{ac + bd + (bc-ad)i}{c^2 + d^2}$ * **Result:** $\frac{ac+bd}{c^2+d^2} + \frac{bc-ad}{c^2+d^2}i$ * **Example:** $\frac{2+3i}{1-i} = \frac{(2+3i)(1+i)}{(1-i)(1+i)} = \frac{2+2i+3i+3i^2}{1-i^2} = \frac{2+5i-3}{1+1} = \frac{-1+5i}{2} = -\frac{1}{2} + \frac{5}{2}i$. ### 4. Quotient in Abstract Algebra In abstract algebra, the concept of a "quotient" extends to creating new algebraic structures (groups, rings, vector spaces) by "dividing out" or "modding out" an equivalence relation defined by a special substructure. This process identifies elements that are "equivalent" to each other. * **Quotient Groups ($G/N$):** * **Context:** Given a group $(G, \cdot)$ and a normal subgroup $N \trianglelefteq G$. A normal subgroup ensures the operation on cosets is well-defined. * **Definition:** The set of left (or right) cosets of $N$ in $G$, denoted $G/N = \{gN \mid g \in G\}$, where $gN = \{gn \mid n \in N\}$. * **Operation:** The product of two cosets $(g_1N) \cdot (g_2N)$ is defined as $(g_1g_2)N$. * **Significance:** $G/N$ forms a group itself, called the quotient group, where elements of $N$ act as the identity. This is crucial for understanding group homomorphisms and the Isomorphism Theorems. * **Example:** The group of integers under addition, $(\mathbb{Z}, +)$, and its subgroup $n\mathbb{Z} = \{..., -2n, -n, 0, n, 2n, ...\}$ (multiples of $n$). The quotient group $\mathbb{Z}/n\mathbb{Z}$ consists of $n$ distinct cosets: $\{0+n\mathbb{Z}, 1+n\mathbb{Z}, ..., (n-1)+n\mathbb{Z}\}$. This is precisely the group of integers modulo $n$, which you encounter in modular arithmetic. * **Quotient Rings ($R/I$):** * **Context:** Given a ring $(R, +, \cdot)$ and an ideal $I \subseteq R$. An ideal is a special subring that plays a role analogous to a normal subgroup in group theory. * **Definition:** The set of cosets $R/I = \{r+I \mid r \in R\}$, where $r+I = \{r+i \mid i \in I\}$. * **Operations:** * $(r_1+I) + (r_2+I) = (r_1+r_2)+I$ * $(r_1+I) \cdot (r_2+I) = (r_1r_2)+I$ * **Significance:** $R/I$ forms a ring itself, called the quotient ring. The elements of $I$ behave like zero in $R/I$. This is fundamental for constructing new rings and understanding ring homomorphisms. * **Example:** Consider the ring of polynomials with real coefficients, $\mathbb{R}[x]$, and the ideal $I = \langle x^2+1 \rangle$, which consists of all polynomials divisible by $x^2+1$. The quotient ring $\mathbb{R}[x]/\langle x^2+1 \rangle$ is isomorphic to the field of complex numbers $\mathbb{C}$. In this ring, $x^2+1 \equiv 0 \pmod{x^2+1}$, which means $x^2 \equiv -1 \pmod{x^2+1}$, effectively making $x$ behave like $i$. * **Quotient Vector Spaces ($V/W$):** * **Context:** Given a vector space $V$ over a field $F$ and a subspace $W \subseteq V$. * **Definition:** The set of cosets $V/W = \{v+W \mid v \in V\}$, where $v+W = \{v+w \mid w \in W\}$. * **Operations:** * Vector addition: $(v_1+W) + (v_2+W) = (v_1+v_2)+W$ * Scalar multiplication: $c(v+W) = (cv)+W$ for $c \in F$. * **Significance:** $V/W$ forms a vector space itself, called the quotient space. Geometrically, it means all vectors differing by an element of $W$ are considered equivalent. It's like "collapsing" the subspace $W$ to the zero vector. * **Example:** In $\mathbb{R}^3$, let $V = \mathbb{R}^3$ and $W$ be the $xy$-plane (a subspace). The quotient space $\mathbb{R}^3/W$ can be thought of as identifying all points that lie on the same vertical line. Each element in $\mathbb{R}^3/W$ is essentially a "height" above the $xy$-plane, behaving like $\mathbb{R}$. ### 5. Applications and Significance The concept of quotient is pervasive in mathematics, engineering, and computer science: * **Modular Arithmetic:** Used extensively in cryptography (RSA, elliptic curve cryptography), error detection codes (checksums), and computer science (hash functions, data structures like circular buffers). * **Control Systems and Signal Processing:** Ratios like gain (output/input), signal-to-noise ratio, and frequency division are direct applications of quotients. * **Computer Science:** Integer division and the modulo operator (`%`) are fundamental for algorithms, array indexing, and generating patterns. * **Physics and Engineering Ratios:** Stress/strain, pressure, density, power-to-weight ratio – all fundamental quantities expressed as quotients. ### 6. Interesting Points / Advanced Concepts * **Modulo Operator (`%`):** Often confused with remainder. While they are usually the same for positive numbers, for negative numbers, the behavior can differ depending on whether the result should have the sign of the dividend (remainder) or the divisor (modulo). The Division Algorithm defines remainder $r$ such that $0 \le r < |b|$, which is consistent across positive and negative dividends. * **Continued Fractions:** These are expressions of numbers as a sum of an integer and the reciprocal of an integer, which is itself a sum of an integer and a reciprocal, and so on. The "integer" parts are essentially successive quotients obtained by applying the Euclidean algorithm. * **Field of Fractions:** For any integral domain (a ring without zero divisors, like integers), one can construct its "field of fractions" (like rational numbers from integers) by forming quotients of elements. This is a powerful construction in algebra. ### Summary of Key Points * A **quotient** is fundamentally the result of division, indicating how many times a divisor fits into a dividend. * The **Division Algorithm** formally defines integer quotient and remainder ($a = bq+r$, $0 \le r < |b|$). * Quotients extend beyond integers to **rational, real, and complex numbers**. * In **abstract algebra**, "quotient" refers to constructing new algebraic structures (groups, rings, vector spaces) by defining an equivalence relation using a special substructure (normal subgroup, ideal, subspace), effectively "modding out" elements that are equivalent. * The concept of quotient is **ubiquitous in engineering and computer science**, from modular arithmetic to ratio calculations and fundamental algorithms. 14.) Pythagorean) The Pythagorean theorem is a fundamental principle in Euclidean geometry, establishing a crucial relationship between the three sides of a right-angled triangle. ### 1. Definition and Historical Context * **Definition**: In a right-angled triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse (the side opposite the right angle) is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides (the legs). * **Formula**: If 'a' and 'b' are the lengths of the legs and 'c' is the length of the hypotenuse, the theorem states: $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ * **Pythagoras of Samos**: Though named after the ancient Greek mathematician Pythagoras (c. 570 – c. 495 BC) and his followers, the Pythagorean Brotherhood, the relationship was known to various ancient civilizations (Babylonians, Egyptians, Chinese) centuries before Pythagoras. The Babylonians, for example, had tablets like Plimpton 322 (circa 1800 BC) demonstrating knowledge of Pythagorean triples. Pythagoras is credited with providing the first known general proof. ### 2. The Right-Angled Triangle * **Right Angle**: A triangle is "right-angled" if one of its interior angles measures exactly 90 degrees ($\pi/2$ radians). * **Sides Nomenclature**: * **Hypotenuse (c)**: The side opposite the right angle. It is always the longest side of a right-angled triangle. * **Legs (a, b)**: The two sides that form the right angle. * **Example**: Consider a right triangle with legs of length 3 units and 4 units. * $a = 3$, $b = 4$ * $c^2 = 3^2 + 4^2 = 9 + 16 = 25$ * $c = \sqrt{25} = 5$ units. ### 3. Proofs of the Theorem There are hundreds of known proofs for the Pythagorean theorem. Here are two classic ones: * **3.1. Geometric Proof (Rearrangement/Area-based)** This proof involves arranging squares and triangles to demonstrate the area relationship. * **Setup**: 1. Start with a right-angled triangle with sides 'a', 'b', and 'c'. 2. Construct a large square with side length $(a+b)$. 3. Inside this large square, arrange four copies of the original right-angled triangle and one central square with side 'c'. * **Derivation**: * The area of the large square is $(a+b)^2$. * This large square's area can also be expressed as the sum of the areas of the four triangles and the inner square: Area = $4 \times (\frac{1}{2}ab) + c^2$ Area = $2ab + c^2$ * Equating the two expressions for the area: $(a+b)^2 = 2ab + c^2$ $a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = 2ab + c^2$ Subtract $2ab$ from both sides: $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ * **Analogy**: Imagine tiling a floor. The total area remains the same regardless of how you arrange the tiles. * **3.2. Algebraic Proof (Using Similar Triangles)** This proof is more rigorous and relies on properties of similar triangles. * **Setup**: 1. Let $\triangle ABC$ be a right-angled triangle with the right angle at C. Let the sides opposite A, B, C be a, b, c respectively. 2. Draw an altitude (height) 'h' from the right angle C to the hypotenuse AB, meeting AB at point D. * **Derivation**: * The altitude divides the original triangle $\triangle ABC$ into two smaller triangles, $\triangle ADC$ and $\triangle BDC$. * Notice that all three triangles ($\triangle ABC$, $\triangle ADC$, $\triangle BDC$) are similar to each other (Angle-Angle similarity criterion, as they all share a right angle and another common angle with the original triangle). * $\triangle ADC \sim \triangle ACB$ (share angle A, both have a 90° angle) * $\triangle BDC \sim \triangle BCA$ (share angle B, both have a 90° angle) * From $\triangle ADC \sim \triangle ACB$: $\frac{AD}{AC} = \frac{AC}{AB} \implies \frac{AD}{b} = \frac{b}{c} \implies b^2 = AD \cdot c$ (Eq. 1) * From $\triangle BDC \sim \triangle BCA$: $\frac{BD}{BC} = \frac{BC}{BA} \implies \frac{BD}{a} = \frac{a}{c} \implies a^2 = BD \cdot c$ (Eq. 2) * Add Eq. 1 and Eq. 2: $a^2 + b^2 = (BD \cdot c) + (AD \cdot c)$ $a^2 + b^2 = c (BD + AD)$ * Since $BD + AD = AB = c$ (the hypotenuse), substitute this into the equation: $a^2 + b^2 = c \cdot c$ $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ ### 4. Converse of the Pythagorean Theorem * **Statement**: If, in a triangle with sides a, b, and c, the relationship $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ holds true, then the angle opposite side 'c' is a right angle (90 degrees), and the triangle is a right-angled triangle. * **Application**: This is extremely useful in construction and surveying to verify if a corner is perfectly square (90 degrees). For example, if you measure a corner and find sides of 3 units and 4 units, and the diagonal measures 5 units, you know it's a perfect right angle. ### 5. Pythagorean Triples * **Definition**: A Pythagorean triple consists of three positive integers (a, b, c) such that $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$. * **Examples**: * (3, 4, 5) - the most famous * (5, 12, 13) * (8, 15, 17) * (7, 24, 25) * **Primitive Triples**: A Pythagorean triple (a, b, c) is called "primitive" if a, b, and c are coprime (their greatest common divisor is 1). (3, 4, 5) is primitive, while (6, 8, 10) is not (it's $2 \times (3, 4, 5)$). * **Euclid's Formula for Generating Triples**: For any two positive integers $m > n > 0$, a Pythagorean triple (a, b, c) can be generated by: * $a = m^2 - n^2$ * $b = 2mn$ * $c = m^2 + n^2$ * **Condition for Primitive Triples**: For (a, b, c) to be primitive, m and n must be coprime, and one of m or n must be even. * **Derivation (Brief)**: This formula arises from considering rational points on the unit circle $x^2 + y^2 = 1$. If we take a line with rational slope 't' passing through (-1, 0), it intersects the circle at another rational point. Parameterizing this leads to $x = (1-t^2)/(1+t^2)$ and $y = 2t/(1+t^2)$. Setting $t=n/m$ and clearing denominators yields Euclid's formula. ### 6. Applications in Engineering and Real World The Pythagorean theorem is indispensable across numerous engineering disciplines: * **Civil Engineering & Construction**: * **Squaring Foundations**: Ensuring building corners are perfectly square. * **Roof Truss Design**: Calculating the lengths of diagonal support beams for pitched roofs. * **Bridge Construction**: Determining cable lengths and structural member dimensions. * **Ramps and Slopes**: Calculating required length or height for a given angle. * **Mechanical Engineering**: * **Vector Analysis**: Calculating the magnitude of a resultant force, velocity, or acceleration from perpendicular components (e.g., $R = \sqrt{F_x^2 + F_y^2}$). * **Robotics**: Calculating distances between robotic arm joints or end-effectors. * **Mechanism Design**: Determining lengths of linkages. * **Electrical Engineering**: * **AC Circuit Analysis**: In impedance triangles, the relationship between resistance (R), reactance (X), and impedance (Z) is $Z^2 = R^2 + X^2$. * **Power Triangles**: Relationship between real power (P), reactive power (Q), and apparent power (S) is $S^2 = P^2 + Q^2$. * **Computer Science & Graphics**: * **Distance Formula**: Calculating the distance between two points in 2D or 3D Cartesian coordinates (an extension of the theorem). * **Game Development**: Collision detection, character movement paths. * **Image Processing**: Calculating gradients or edge magnitudes. * **Surveying & Navigation**: * **Land Measurement**: Calculating distances and heights across uneven terrain. * **GPS Systems**: Calculating distances between GPS satellites and receivers to pinpoint locations. * **Dead Reckoning**: Estimating position based on speed, course, and time. * **Aerospace Engineering**: * **Flight Path Calculation**: Determining actual ground speed from airspeed and wind speed components. * **Trajectory Analysis**: Calculating projectile range and height. ### 7. Generalizations and Extensions * **Law of Cosines**: The Pythagorean theorem is a special case of the Law of Cosines. For any triangle with sides a, b, c and angle $\gamma$ opposite side c: $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(\gamma)$ If the angle $\gamma$ is a right angle (90°), then $\cos(90^\circ) = 0$, and the formula simplifies to $c^2 = a^2 + b^2$. * **Distance Formula in N-Dimensions**: The theorem extends naturally to higher dimensions for calculating distances. * In 2D: Distance $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$ * In 3D: Distance $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2 + (z_2 - z_1)^2}$ This can be seen as applying the Pythagorean theorem iteratively. * **Inner Product Spaces**: In more abstract linear algebra, the Pythagorean theorem holds for orthogonal vectors in an inner product space. If vectors **u** and **v** are orthogonal (their inner product is zero), then $||u+v||^2 = ||u||^2 + ||v||^2$. ### 8. Interesting Points * **Irrational Numbers**: The discovery by the Pythagoreans that the hypotenuse of a right triangle with legs of length 1 unit (i.e., $\sqrt{2}$) could not be expressed as a ratio of two integers (an irrational number) was a profound mathematical crisis at the time, challenging their belief in the supremacy of rational numbers. * **Visual Intuition**: Many proofs of the Pythagorean theorem are "dissection proofs" that demonstrate the area relationship visually, making it one of the most intuitively understood theorems in geometry. ### Summary of Key Points * The Pythagorean theorem, $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$, applies exclusively to right-angled triangles. * 'a' and 'b' are legs, 'c' is the hypotenuse (always the longest side). * Multiple proofs exist, including geometric (area rearrangement) and algebraic (similar triangles). * Its converse allows us to determine if a triangle is right-angled. * Pythagorean triples are integer solutions to the equation, and Euclid's formula generates them. * The theorem is a cornerstone for distance calculations and vector analysis in various engineering fields (Civil, Mechanical, Electrical, Computer Science, etc.). * It generalizes to the Law of Cosines and higher-dimensional distance formulas, demonstrating its pervasive nature in mathematics. 15.) Sum and Difference Identities Sum and Difference Identities are fundamental formulas in trigonometry that allow us to express the sine, cosine, or tangent of a sum or difference of two angles in terms of the sines and cosines of the individual angles. These identities are crucial for simplifying expressions, solving equations, and deriving other trigonometric formulas, especially in fields like physics and engineering for analyzing waves, oscillations, and signals. ### 1. What are Sum and Difference Identities? These identities essentially answer questions like: If you know $\sin(A)$, $\cos(A)$, $\sin(B)$, and $\cos(B)$, can you find $\sin(A+B)$ or $\cos(A-B)$? Yes, you can, using specific formulas. They are a powerful tool to break down complex angular relationships into simpler ones. ### 2. Derivation Strategy: The Cosine of a Difference ($\cos(A-B)$) All other sum and difference identities are derived from the Cosine of a Difference identity. The most common and robust proof involves using the **Unit Circle** and the **Distance Formula**. **Proof of $\cos(A-B)$:** Consider a unit circle (radius = 1) centered at the origin (0,0). Let $A$ and $B$ be two angles in standard position. * Point $P_1$ on the circle corresponding to angle $A$: $P_1 = (\cos A, \sin A)$. * Point $P_2$ on the circle corresponding to angle $B$: $P_2 = (\cos B, \sin B)$. * The angle between $OP_1$ and $OP_2$ is $(A-B)$. Now, rotate the entire configuration so that $P_2$ moves to point $P_4(1,0)$ (which corresponds to angle 0). * The angle $A-B$ will now be in standard position. * The new position of $P_1$ will be $P_3(\cos(A-B), \sin(A-B))$. * The new position of $P_2$ is $P_4(1,0)$. Since rotation preserves distance, the distance between $P_1$ and $P_2$ must be equal to the distance between $P_3$ and $P_4$. Using the distance formula $d = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$: Distance $(P_1P_2)^2$: $= (\cos B - \cos A)^2 + (\sin B - \sin A)^2$ $= (\cos^2 B - 2\cos A \cos B + \cos^2 A) + (\sin^2 B - 2\sin A \sin B + \sin^2 A)$ $= (\cos^2 A + \sin^2 A) + (\cos^2 B + \sin^2 B) - 2\cos A \cos B - 2\sin A \sin B$ $= 1 + 1 - 2\cos A \cos B - 2\sin A \sin B$ $= 2 - 2\cos A \cos B - 2\sin A \sin B$ Distance $(P_3P_4)^2$: $= (\cos(A-B) - 1)^2 + (\sin(A-B) - 0)^2$ $= \cos^2(A-B) - 2\cos(A-B) + 1 + \sin^2(A-B)$ $= (\cos^2(A-B) + \sin^2(A-B)) + 1 - 2\cos(A-B)$ $= 1 + 1 - 2\cos(A-B)$ $= 2 - 2\cos(A-B)$ Equating $(P_1P_2)^2$ and $(P_3P_4)^2$: $2 - 2\cos A \cos B - 2\sin A \sin B = 2 - 2\cos(A-B)$ $-2\cos A \cos B - 2\sin A \sin B = -2\cos(A-B)$ Dividing by -2: $\boxed{\cos(A-B) = \cos A \cos B + \sin A \sin B}$ ### 3. Core Identities: Derivations Once $\cos(A-B)$ is established, the other five can be derived from it. * **Cosine of a Sum: $\cos(A+B)$** We can rewrite $A+B$ as $A-(-B)$. Apply the $\cos(X-Y)$ formula where $X=A$ and $Y=-B$. $\cos(A+B) = \cos(A - (-B))$ $= \cos A \cos(-B) + \sin A \sin(-B)$ Recall: $\cos(-B) = \cos B$ (cosine is an even function) Recall: $\sin(-B) = -\sin B$ (sine is an odd function) $\boxed{\cos(A+B) = \cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B}$ * **Sine of a Sum: $\sin(A+B)$** We use the co-function identity: $\sin \theta = \cos(90^\circ - \theta)$ or $\sin \theta = \cos(\frac{\pi}{2} - \theta)$. Let $\theta = A+B$. $\sin(A+B) = \cos(90^\circ - (A+B))$ $= \cos((90^\circ - A) - B)$ Now apply the $\cos(X-Y)$ formula where $X = (90^\circ - A)$ and $Y=B$. $= \cos(90^\circ - A) \cos B + \sin(90^\circ - A) \sin B$ Recall: $\cos(90^\circ - A) = \sin A$ and $\sin(90^\circ - A) = \cos A$. $\boxed{\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B}$ * **Sine of a Difference: $\sin(A-B)$** Rewrite $A-B$ as $A+(-B)$. Apply the $\sin(X+Y)$ formula where $X=A$ and $Y=-B$. $\sin(A-B) = \sin(A + (-B))$ $= \sin A \cos(-B) + \cos A \sin(-B)$ Using $\cos(-B) = \cos B$ and $\sin(-B) = -\sin B$: $\boxed{\sin(A-B) = \sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B}$ * **Tangent of a Sum: $\tan(A+B)$** Use the identity $\tan \theta = \frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta}$. $\tan(A+B) = \frac{\sin(A+B)}{\cos(A+B)}$ $= \frac{\sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B}{\cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B}$ To simplify, divide every term in the numerator and denominator by $\cos A \cos B$ (assuming $\cos A \cos B \neq 0$). $= \frac{\frac{\sin A \cos B}{\cos A \cos B} + \frac{\cos A \sin B}{\cos A \cos B}}{\frac{\cos A \cos B}{\cos A \cos B} - \frac{\sin A \sin B}{\cos A \cos B}}$ $= \frac{\frac{\sin A}{\cos A} + \frac{\sin B}{\cos B}}{1 - \frac{\sin A}{\cos A} \frac{\sin B}{\cos B}}$ $\boxed{\tan(A+B) = \frac{\tan A + \tan B}{1 - \tan A \tan B}}$ * **Tangent of a Difference: $\tan(A-B)$** Similar to the sum, replace $B$ with $-B$ in the $\tan(A+B)$ formula. $\tan(A-B) = \tan(A + (-B))$ $= \frac{\tan A + \tan(-B)}{1 - \tan A \tan(-B)}$ Recall: $\tan(-B) = -\tan B$ (tangent is an odd function). $\boxed{\tan(A-B) = \frac{\tan A - \tan B}{1 + \tan A \tan B}}$ ### 4. Summary of Sum and Difference Identities 1. $\cos(A+B) = \cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B$ 2. $\cos(A-B) = \cos A \cos B + \sin A \sin B$ 3. $\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B$ 4. $\sin(A-B) = \sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B$ 5. $\tan(A+B) = \frac{\tan A + \tan B}{1 - \tan A \tan B}$ 6. $\tan(A-B) = \frac{\tan A - \tan B}{1 + \tan A \tan B}$ ### 5. Applications and Examples * **Finding Exact Values:** **Example:** Find the exact value of $\cos(75^\circ)$. We can write $75^\circ$ as a sum or difference of common angles (e.g., $45^\circ + 30^\circ$). $\cos(75^\circ) = \cos(45^\circ + 30^\circ)$ Using $\cos(A+B) = \cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B$: $= \cos(45^\circ)\cos(30^\circ) - \sin(45^\circ)\sin(30^\circ)$ $= \left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right) - \left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)$ $= \frac{\sqrt{6}}{4} - \frac{\sqrt{2}}{4} = \frac{\sqrt{6}-\sqrt{2}}{4}$ * **Simplifying Trigonometric Expressions:** **Example:** Simplify $\sin(x + \frac{\pi}{4}) + \sin(x - \frac{\pi}{4})$. Using $\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B$ and $\sin(A-B) = \sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B$: $\sin(x + \frac{\pi}{4}) = \sin x \cos(\frac{\pi}{4}) + \cos x \sin(\frac{\pi}{4}) = \sin x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} + \cos x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ $\sin(x - \frac{\pi}{4}) = \sin x \cos(\frac{\pi}{4}) - \cos x \sin(\frac{\pi}{4}) = \sin x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} - \cos x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$ Adding them: $(\sin x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} + \cos x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}) + (\sin x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} - \cos x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2})$ $= 2 \sin x \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} = \sqrt{2} \sin x$ * **Proving Other Identities:** **Example:** Prove $\cos(2A) = \cos^2 A - \sin^2 A$. This is a double-angle identity, which can be derived from sum identities. $\cos(2A) = \cos(A+A)$ Using $\cos(X+Y) = \cos X \cos Y - \sin X \sin Y$ with $X=A, Y=A$: $= \cos A \cos A - \sin A \sin A$ $= \cos^2 A - \sin^2 A$ ### 6. Extra Interesting Points and Connections * **Complex Numbers (Euler's Formula):** For engineering students, a powerful connection exists via Euler's formula: $e^{i\theta} = \cos \theta + i \sin \theta$. Then, $e^{i(A+B)} = \cos(A+B) + i \sin(A+B)$. Also, $e^{i(A+B)} = e^{iA}e^{iB} = (\cos A + i \sin A)(\cos B + i \sin B)$. Expanding the product: $(\cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B) + i(\sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B)$. Equating real and imaginary parts directly yields the cosine and sine sum identities, offering an elegant proof using complex exponentials. This is highly relevant in electrical engineering (phasors, AC circuits). * **Wave Interference and Superposition:** In physics and engineering, especially in signal processing, acoustics, and optics, waves are often represented by sinusoidal functions. When two waves combine (superimpose), their resultant can be analyzed using sum and difference identities. For example, understanding beat frequencies or phase shifts involves these trigonometric relationships. * **Derivation of Product-to-Sum and Sum-to-Product Formulas:** These identities are the stepping stones for proving more advanced formulas used in Fourier analysis and digital signal processing, which are critical in engineering. ### 7. Summary of Key Points * Sum and Difference Identities express trigonometric functions of combined angles (A±B) in terms of functions of individual angles (A and B). * The derivation of $\cos(A-B)$ using the unit circle and distance formula is fundamental. * All other five identities ($\cos(A+B), \sin(A+B), \sin(A-B), \tan(A+B), \tan(A-B)$) can be systematically derived from $\cos(A-B)$. * They are essential tools for finding exact values for non-standard angles, simplifying complex trigonometric expressions, and proving other identities (e.g., double-angle, half-angle). * These identities have deep connections to complex numbers (Euler's formula) and practical applications in engineering fields like signal processing and wave phenomena. 16.) Double and Half-Angle Identities ### Double and Half-Angle Identities Double and Half-Angle Identities are fundamental trigonometric formulas that relate trigonometric functions of an angle to trigonometric functions of twice or half that angle. They are indispensable tools in simplifying expressions, solving trigonometric equations, and are widely applied in calculus, physics, and engineering for transforming and manipulating trigonometric forms. Before diving in, recall the **Sum and Difference Identities** as they are the building blocks: * $\sin(A \pm B) = \sin A \cos B \pm \cos A \sin B$ * $\cos(A \pm B) = \cos A \cos B \mp \sin A \sin B$ * $\tan(A \pm B) = \frac{\tan A \pm \tan B}{1 \mp \tan A \tan B}$ --- ### 1. Double-Angle Identities These identities express trigonometric functions of $2A$ in terms of trigonometric functions of $A$. #### a) Sine Double-Angle Identity: $\sin(2A)$ * **Derivation:** Start with the sum identity for sine: $\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B$. Let $B=A$. $\sin(A+A) = \sin A \cos A + \cos A \sin A$ $\sin(2A) = 2 \sin A \cos A$ * **Definition:** The sine of twice an angle is twice the product of the sine and cosine of the angle. * **Example:** If $\sin A = 3/5$ and $A$ is in Quadrant I, find $\sin(2A)$. First, find $\cos A = \sqrt{1 - (3/5)^2} = 4/5$. $\sin(2A) = 2 \sin A \cos A = 2(3/5)(4/5) = 24/25$. #### b) Cosine Double-Angle Identities: $\cos(2A)$ These are particularly useful because there are three primary forms, allowing flexibility in problem-solving. * **Derivation (Form 1):** Start with the sum identity for cosine: $\cos(A+B) = \cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B$. Let $B=A$. $\cos(A+A) = \cos A \cos A - \sin A \sin A$ $\cos(2A) = \cos^2 A - \sin^2 A$ * **Derivation (Form 2 - in terms of $\cos A$):** Use the Pythagorean Identity: $\sin^2 A = 1 - \cos^2 A$. Substitute into Form 1: $\cos(2A) = \cos^2 A - (1 - \cos^2 A)$ $\cos(2A) = 2 \cos^2 A - 1$ * **Derivation (Form 3 - in terms of $\sin A$):** Use the Pythagorean Identity: $\cos^2 A = 1 - \sin^2 A$. Substitute into Form 1: $\cos(2A) = (1 - \sin^2 A) - \sin^2 A$ $\cos(2A) = 1 - 2 \sin^2 A$ * **Summary of Forms:** 1. $\cos(2A) = \cos^2 A - \sin^2 A$ 2. $\cos(2A) = 2 \cos^2 A - 1$ 3. $\cos(2A) = 1 - 2 \sin^2 A$ * **Example:** If $\cos A = 1/3$, find $\cos(2A)$. Using Form 2: $\cos(2A) = 2 \cos^2 A - 1 = 2(1/3)^2 - 1 = 2(1/9) - 1 = 2/9 - 1 = -7/9$. #### c) Tangent Double-Angle Identity: $\tan(2A)$ * **Derivation:** Start with the sum identity for tangent: $\tan(A+B) = \frac{\tan A + \tan B}{1 - \tan A \tan B}$. Let $B=A$. $\tan(A+A) = \frac{\tan A + \tan A}{1 - \tan A \tan A}$ $\tan(2A) = \frac{2 \tan A}{1 - \tan^2 A}$ * **Definition:** The tangent of twice an angle is twice the tangent of the angle divided by one minus the square of the tangent of the angle. * **Example:** If $\tan A = 2$, find $\tan(2A)$. $\tan(2A) = \frac{2(2)}{1 - (2)^2} = \frac{4}{1 - 4} = \frac{4}{-3} = -4/3$. --- ### 2. Half-Angle Identities These identities express trigonometric functions of $A/2$ in terms of trigonometric functions of $A$. They are derived directly from the cosine double-angle identities by rearranging and substituting. #### a) Sine Half-Angle Identity: $\sin(A/2)$ * **Derivation:** Start with the cosine double-angle identity: $\cos(2x) = 1 - 2 \sin^2 x$. Let $2x = A$, which means $x = A/2$. $\cos A = 1 - 2 \sin^2 (A/2)$ Rearrange to solve for $\sin^2 (A/2)$: $2 \sin^2 (A/2) = 1 - \cos A$ $\sin^2 (A/2) = \frac{1 - \cos A}{2}$ $\sin(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{2}}$ * **Sign Ambiguity:** The $\pm$ sign depends on the quadrant in which $A/2$ lies. If $A/2$ is in Quadrant I or II, $\sin(A/2)$ is positive. If $A/2$ is in Quadrant III or IV, $\sin(A/2)$ is negative. * **Example:** Find $\sin(15^\circ)$. We know $\cos(30^\circ) = \sqrt{3}/2$. Here, $A = 30^\circ$, so $A/2 = 15^\circ$. Since $15^\circ$ is in Quadrant I, $\sin(15^\circ)$ is positive. $\sin(15^\circ) = \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos(30^\circ)}{2}} = \sqrt{\frac{1 - \sqrt{3}/2}{2}} = \sqrt{\frac{2 - \sqrt{3}}{4}} = \frac{\sqrt{2 - \sqrt{3}}}{2}$. #### b) Cosine Half-Angle Identity: $\cos(A/2)$ * **Derivation:** Start with the cosine double-angle identity: $\cos(2x) = 2 \cos^2 x - 1$. Let $2x = A$, so $x = A/2$. $\cos A = 2 \cos^2 (A/2) - 1$ Rearrange to solve for $\cos^2 (A/2)$: $2 \cos^2 (A/2) = 1 + \cos A$ $\cos^2 (A/2) = \frac{1 + \cos A}{2}$ $\cos(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos A}{2}}$ * **Sign Ambiguity:** The $\pm$ sign depends on the quadrant in which $A/2$ lies. If $A/2$ is in Quadrant I or IV, $\cos(A/2)$ is positive. If $A/2$ is in Quadrant II or III, $\cos(A/2)$ is negative. * **Example:** Find $\cos(105^\circ)$. We know $\cos(210^\circ) = -\sqrt{3}/2$. Here, $A = 210^\circ$, so $A/2 = 105^\circ$. Since $105^\circ$ is in Quadrant II, $\cos(105^\circ)$ is negative. $\cos(105^\circ) = -\sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos(210^\circ)}{2}} = -\sqrt{\frac{1 + (-\sqrt{3}/2)}{2}} = -\sqrt{\frac{2 - \sqrt{3}}{4}} = -\frac{\sqrt{2 - \sqrt{3}}}{2}$. #### c) Tangent Half-Angle Identities: $\tan(A/2)$ These have multiple convenient forms. * **Derivation (Form 1):** Use $\tan(A/2) = \frac{\sin(A/2)}{\cos(A/2)}$. $\tan(A/2) = \frac{\pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{2}}}{\pm \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos A}{2}}} = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{1 + \cos A}}$ The sign depends on the quadrant of $A/2$. * **Derivation (Form 2 - Preferred for algebraic simplicity):** Multiply numerator and denominator of Form 1 by $\sin(A/2)$ (or use $\tan(A/2) = \frac{\sin(A/2)}{\cos(A/2)}$ and multiply by $\frac{2 \cos(A/2)}{2 \cos(A/2)}$ and then by $\frac{1-\cos A}{1-\cos A}$ inside the square root, it's simpler to show via algebraic manipulation of the initial half angle formulas) Consider $\tan(A/2) = \frac{1 - \cos A}{\sin A}$: We know $\sin A = 2 \sin(A/2)\cos(A/2)$ and $1-\cos A = 2 \sin^2(A/2)$. So, $\frac{1-\cos A}{\sin A} = \frac{2 \sin^2(A/2)}{2 \sin(A/2)\cos(A/2)} = \frac{\sin(A/2)}{\cos(A/2)} = \tan(A/2)$. * **Derivation (Form 3 - Also Preferred):** Consider $\tan(A/2) = \frac{\sin A}{1 + \cos A}$: We know $\sin A = 2 \sin(A/2)\cos(A/2)$ and $1+\cos A = 2 \cos^2(A/2)$. So, $\frac{\sin A}{1+\cos A} = \frac{2 \sin(A/2)\cos(A/2)}{2 \cos^2(A/2)} = \frac{\sin(A/2)}{\cos(A/2)} = \tan(A/2)$. * **Summary of Forms:** 1. $\tan(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{1 + \cos A}}$ 2. $\tan(A/2) = \frac{1 - \cos A}{\sin A}$ 3. $\tan(A/2) = \frac{\sin A}{1 + \cos A}$ Forms 2 and 3 are often preferred as they do not require determining the quadrant of $A/2$ for the sign. * **Example:** Find $\tan(22.5^\circ)$. We know $\cos(45^\circ) = \sqrt{2}/2$ and $\sin(45^\circ) = \sqrt{2}/2$. Using Form 2: $\tan(22.5^\circ) = \frac{1 - \cos(45^\circ)}{\sin(45^\circ)} = \frac{1 - \sqrt{2}/2}{\sqrt{2}/2} = \frac{(2 - \sqrt{2})/2}{\sqrt{2}/2} = \frac{2 - \sqrt{2}}{\sqrt{2}}$. Rationalize: $\frac{(2 - \sqrt{2})\sqrt{2}}{2} = \frac{2\sqrt{2} - 2}{2} = \sqrt{2} - 1$. --- ### 3. Applications and Importance * **Simplifying Expressions:** These identities are crucial for reducing complex trigonometric expressions to simpler forms. * Example: Simplify $\frac{\sin(2x)}{1+\cos(2x)}$. $\frac{2\sin x \cos x}{1+(2\cos^2 x - 1)} = \frac{2\sin x \cos x}{2\cos^2 x} = \frac{\sin x}{\cos x} = \tan x$. * **Solving Trigonometric Equations:** They help transform equations involving multiple angles or half-angles into equations with a single angle, making them solvable. * Example: Solve $\sin(2x) = \cos x$. $2\sin x \cos x = \cos x$ $2\sin x \cos x - \cos x = 0$ $\cos x (2\sin x - 1) = 0$ So, $\cos x = 0 \implies x = \pi/2 + n\pi$ or $2\sin x - 1 = 0 \implies \sin x = 1/2 \implies x = \pi/6 + 2n\pi$ or $x = 5\pi/6 + 2n\pi$. * **Calculus:** Essential for integration (e.g., transforming $\sin^2 x$ into a form suitable for integration using power-reducing formulas, which are rearrangements of cosine double-angle identities) and differentiation. * Power-reducing formulas: $\sin^2 A = \frac{1 - \cos(2A)}{2}$ and $\cos^2 A = \frac{1 + \cos(2A)}{2}$. These are direct rearrangements of the cosine double-angle identities. * **Physics and Engineering:** Used in wave analysis, signal processing, and describing oscillatory motion, where manipulating angles is frequently required. --- ### 4. Extra Interesting Points * **Connection to Complex Numbers:** Double and half-angle identities can be elegantly derived using Euler's formula, $e^{i\theta} = \cos\theta + i\sin\theta$. For example, $e^{i2\theta} = (e^{i\theta})^2 \implies \cos(2\theta) + i\sin(2\theta) = (\cos\theta + i\sin\theta)^2 = (\cos^2\theta - \sin^2\theta) + i(2\sin\theta\cos\theta)$. Equating real and imaginary parts immediately gives the double-angle identities for cosine and sine. This demonstrates their fundamental nature. * **Generalization:** These identities are specific cases of more general multiple-angle formulas (e.g., for $\sin(nA)$, $\cos(nA)$) and product-to-sum/sum-to-product identities, highlighting the interconnectedness of trigonometric relations. --- ### Summary of Key Points * **Double-Angle Identities** relate trigonometric functions of $2A$ to $A$: * $\sin(2A) = 2 \sin A \cos A$ * $\cos(2A) = \cos^2 A - \sin^2 A = 2 \cos^2 A - 1 = 1 - 2 \sin^2 A$ * $\tan(2A) = \frac{2 \tan A}{1 - \tan^2 A}$ * **Half-Angle Identities** relate trigonometric functions of $A/2$ to $A$: * $\sin(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{2}}$ * $\cos(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 + \cos A}{2}}$ * $\tan(A/2) = \pm \sqrt{\frac{1 - \cos A}{1 + \cos A}} = \frac{1 - \cos A}{\sin A} = \frac{\sin A}{1 + \cos A}$ * The choice of $\pm$ for half-angle identities depends on the quadrant of $A/2$. * These identities are derived from the sum/difference identities and Pythagorean identities. * They are powerful tools for simplifying expressions, solving equations, and form the basis for many higher-level mathematical and engineering applications. 17.) Product-to-Sum and Sum-to-Product Identities The Product-to-Sum and Sum-to-Product identities are powerful trigonometric tools that allow us to transform expressions involving products of sine and cosine functions into sums or differences, and vice-versa. These transformations are invaluable in simplifying complex trigonometric expressions, solving equations, and particularly in calculus for integration, as well as in fields like physics and engineering for analyzing waves and signals. ### 1. Prerequisite: Angle Sum and Difference Formulas These fundamental identities are the building blocks for deriving the product-to-sum formulas. Recall them: * $\sin(A+B) = \sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B$ * $\sin(A-B) = \sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B$ * $\cos(A+B) = \cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B$ * $\cos(A-B) = \cos A \cos B + \sin A \sin B$ ### 2. Product-to-Sum Identities **Definition:** These identities convert products of trigonometric functions (like $\sin A \cos B$) into sums or differences of trigonometric functions. This is often useful for integration, as integrals of sums are simpler than integrals of products. **Derivation:** We derive these by judiciously adding or subtracting the angle sum and difference formulas. * **For $2 \sin A \cos B$:** Add $\sin(A+B)$ and $\sin(A-B)$: $(\sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B) + (\sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B)$ $= 2 \sin A \cos B$ Therefore: $\boxed{2 \sin A \cos B = \sin(A+B) + \sin(A-B)}$ * **For $2 \cos A \sin B$:** Subtract $\sin(A-B)$ from $\sin(A+B)$: $(\sin A \cos B + \cos A \sin B) - (\sin A \cos B - \cos A \sin B)$ $= 2 \cos A \sin B$ Therefore: $\boxed{2 \cos A \sin B = \sin(A+B) - \sin(A-B)}$ * **For $2 \cos A \cos B$:** Add $\cos(A+B)$ and $\cos(A-B)$: $(\cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B) + (\cos A \cos B + \sin A \sin B)$ $= 2 \cos A \cos B$ Therefore: $\boxed{2 \cos A \cos B = \cos(A+B) + \cos(A-B)}$ * **For $2 \sin A \sin B$:** Subtract $\cos(A+B)$ from $\cos(A-B)$: $(\cos A \cos B + \sin A \sin B) - (\cos A \cos B - \sin A \sin B)$ $= 2 \sin A \sin B$ Therefore: $\boxed{2 \sin A \sin B = \cos(A-B) - \cos(A+B)}$ **Summary of Product-to-Sum Identities:** 1. $2 \sin A \cos B = \sin(A+B) + \sin(A-B)$ 2. $2 \cos A \sin B = \sin(A+B) - \sin(A-B)$ 3. $2 \cos A \cos B = \cos(A+B) + \cos(A-B)$ 4. $2 \sin A \sin B = \cos(A-B) - \cos(A+B)$ **Example 1: Simplify $\sin(5x) \cos(3x)$** Using the first identity ($2 \sin A \cos B = \sin(A+B) + \sin(A-B)$), with $A=5x$ and $B=3x$: $\sin(5x) \cos(3x) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(5x+3x) + \sin(5x-3x)]$ $= \frac{1}{2} [\sin(8x) + \sin(2x)]$ **Example 2: How it aids Integration (Engineering Context)** Consider integrating $\int \sin(7x) \sin(3x) dx$. This product is hard to integrate directly. Using the fourth identity ($2 \sin A \sin B = \cos(A-B) - \cos(A+B)$), with $A=7x, B=3x$: $\sin(7x) \sin(3x) = \frac{1}{2} [\cos(7x-3x) - \cos(7x+3x)]$ $= \frac{1}{2} [\cos(4x) - \cos(10x)]$ Now the integral becomes: $\int \frac{1}{2} [\cos(4x) - \cos(10x)] dx = \frac{1}{2} \left[ \frac{\sin(4x)}{4} - \frac{\sin(10x)}{10} \right] + C$ This is much simpler! ### 3. Sum-to-Product Identities **Definition:** These identities convert sums or differences of trigonometric functions (like $\sin X + \sin Y$) into products. This is especially useful for factoring expressions, solving trigonometric equations, and proving other identities. **Derivation:** We derive these by setting $A+B = X$ and $A-B = Y$ in the product-to-sum identities. From these substitutions, we can solve for A and B: Adding the two equations: $(A+B) + (A-B) = X+Y \Rightarrow 2A = X+Y \Rightarrow A = \frac{X+Y}{2}$ Subtracting the two equations: $(A+B) - (A-B) = X-Y \Rightarrow 2B = X-Y \Rightarrow B = \frac{X-Y}{2}$ Now substitute $A$, $B$, $A+B$, and $A-B$ back into the Product-to-Sum identities: * From $2 \sin A \cos B = \sin(A+B) + \sin(A-B)$: $\boxed{\sin X + \sin Y = 2 \sin\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)}$ * From $2 \cos A \sin B = \sin(A+B) - \sin(A-B)$: $\boxed{\sin X - \sin Y = 2 \cos\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)}$ * From $2 \cos A \cos B = \cos(A+B) + \cos(A-B)$: $\boxed{\cos X + \cos Y = 2 \cos\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)}$ * From $2 \sin A \sin B = \cos(A-B) - \cos(A+B)$: Note the order change for $\cos X - \cos Y$: $\cos(A-B) - \cos(A+B) = 2 \sin A \sin B$ $\cos Y - \cos X = 2 \sin\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$ Therefore: $\boxed{\cos X - \cos Y = -2 \sin\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)}$ **Summary of Sum-to-Product Identities:** 1. $\sin X + \sin Y = 2 \sin\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$ 2. $\sin X - \sin Y = 2 \cos\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$ 3. $\cos X + \cos Y = 2 \cos\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$ 4. $\cos X - \cos Y = -2 \sin\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$ **Example 1: Factor $\sin(7x) - \sin(3x)$** Using the second identity ($\sin X - \sin Y = 2 \cos\left(\frac{X+Y}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{X-Y}{2}\right)$), with $X=7x, Y=3x$: $\sin(7x) - \sin(3x) = 2 \cos\left(\frac{7x+3x}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{7x-3x}{2}\right)$ $= 2 \cos\left(\frac{10x}{2}\right) \sin\left(\frac{4x}{2}\right)$ $= 2 \cos(5x) \sin(2x)$ **Example 2: Prove the identity $\frac{\sin A + \sin B}{\cos A + \cos B} = \tan\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$** Using the sum-to-product identities for the numerator and denominator: Numerator: $\sin A + \sin B = 2 \sin\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$ Denominator: $\cos A + \cos B = 2 \cos\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$ Now divide them: $\frac{2 \sin\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)}{2 \cos\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cos\left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)}$ Cancel $2$ and $\cos\left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$: $= \frac{\sin\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)}{\cos\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)} = \tan\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$. Proved. ### 4. Applications and Interesting Points * **Calculus:** As seen in Example 2 for Product-to-Sum, these identities simplify integrals of products, a common task in engineering analysis. * **Signal Processing & Physics (Beat Frequencies):** When two sound waves (or any waves) of slightly different frequencies interfere, they produce "beats." This phenomenon is mathematically described by the product-to-sum identity. For example, $2 \cos(f_1 t) \cos(f_2 t) = \cos((f_1+f_2)t) + \cos((f_1-f_2)t)$. The term $\cos((f_1-f_2)t)$ represents the low-frequency "beat" envelope, while $\cos((f_1+f_2)t)$ is the high-frequency carrier. * **Solving Trigonometric Equations:** Transforming sums or products into other forms can reveal common factors or simplify the equation to a solvable form. * **Fourier Series & Transforms:** While not directly used in the definition, these identities underpin the ability to represent complex periodic signals as sums of simple sines and cosines (Fourier Series), and transforming between time and frequency domains (Fourier Transform), which are critical concepts in electrical engineering and signal processing. * **Proving Other Identities:** As shown in Example 2 for Sum-to-Product, they are powerful tools for establishing more complex trigonometric relationships. ### Summary of Key Points * **Product-to-Sum Identities** transform products of sines and cosines into sums or differences. They are particularly useful for simplifying integrals. * **Sum-to-Product Identities** transform sums or differences of sines and cosines into products. They are great for factoring, solving equations, and proving identities. * Both sets of identities are **derived directly from the fundamental angle sum and difference formulas**, highlighting the interconnectedness of trigonometric identities. * Beyond pure mathematics, they find significant application in **calculus, physics (wave phenomena), and various engineering fields** (e.g., signal processing). 18.) Law of Sines The Law of Sines is a fundamental principle in trigonometry that establishes a relationship between the sides of a triangle and the sines of its opposite angles. It is incredibly useful for solving non-right triangles (oblique triangles) when you have certain combinations of side lengths and angle measures. ### 1. Standard Triangle Notation Before diving into the law, let's establish standard notation for any triangle ABC: * The vertices (corners) are denoted by capital letters: A, B, C. * The angles at these vertices are also denoted by the same capital letters: A, B, C (or $\alpha, \beta, \gamma$). * The side opposite angle A is denoted by lowercase 'a'. * The side opposite angle B is denoted by lowercase 'b'. * The side opposite angle C is denoted by lowercase 'c'. ### 2. The Law of Sines Formula The Law of Sines states that for any triangle ABC: $$\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$$ This means that the ratio of the length of a side to the sine of its opposite angle is constant for all three sides of a given triangle. You can use any two parts of this equality to solve for unknowns. ### 3. Derivation/Proof of the Law of Sines The law can be derived using the concept of the altitude (height) of a triangle. **Step 1: Draw an altitude** Consider an acute triangle ABC. Draw an altitude (height) `h` from vertex C to side AB, meeting AB at point D. * In right triangle ADC: $\sin A = \frac{h}{b} \implies h = b \sin A$ * In right triangle BDC: $\sin B = \frac{h}{a} \implies h = a \sin B$ **Step 2: Equate the expressions for `h`** Since both expressions equal `h`: $b \sin A = a \sin B$ **Step 3: Rearrange to form part of the law** Divide both sides by $(\sin A \cdot \sin B)$: $\frac{b \sin A}{\sin A \sin B} = \frac{a \sin B}{\sin A \sin B}$ $\frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{a}{\sin A}$ **Step 4: Extend to the third side** You can repeat this process by drawing an altitude from vertex A to side BC (or from B to AC). For example, draw an altitude `h'` from A to BC. * From triangle ABH': $\sin B = \frac{h'}{c} \implies h' = c \sin B$ * From triangle ACH': $\sin C = \frac{h'}{b} \implies h' = b \sin C$ Equating these: $c \sin B = b \sin C$ Rearranging: $\frac{c}{\sin C} = \frac{b}{\sin B}$ **Conclusion of Proof:** Combining the derived relations: $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B}$ and $\frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$ Therefore, $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$. **Applicability to Obtuse Triangles:** The derivation holds true for obtuse triangles as well. If an angle is obtuse, the altitude might fall outside the triangle. However, the sine function handles angles up to 180 degrees, and the geometric relationships still hold. For example, if angle B is obtuse, $\sin(\pi - B) = \sin B$. ### 4. When to Use the Law of Sines (Applications) The Law of Sines is primarily used when you know: * **AAS (Angle-Angle-Side):** Two angles and any non-included side. * *Example:* Given A, B, and a. You can find C (since A+B+C=180°), then use the Law of Sines to find b and c. * **ASA (Angle-Side-Angle):** Two angles and the included side. * *Example:* Given A, c, and B. You can find C, then use the Law of Sines to find a and b. * **SSA (Side-Side-Angle): The Ambiguous Case:** Two sides and a non-included angle. This case is "ambiguous" because it can lead to zero, one, or two possible triangles. ### 5. The Ambiguous Case (SSA) - In Detail This is crucial for engineering students as it requires careful analysis. Given sides `a`, `b`, and angle `A` (where `A` is opposite `a`): Let `h` be the altitude from vertex C to side AB. So, $h = b \sin A$. * **Case 1: Angle A is Acute ($A < 90^\circ$)** * **a < h (a < b sin A):** No triangle exists. The side 'a' is too short to reach the base. * **a = h (a = b sin A):** Exactly one right-angled triangle. Side 'a' forms a perpendicular with the base. * **h < a < b (b sin A < a < b):** Two distinct triangles. Side 'a' can swing inward or outward to touch the base, forming two possible positions. This is the "ambiguous" scenario. * **a $\ge$ b:** Exactly one triangle. If a is longer than or equal to b, it can only form one triangle (the other "swing" would create a triangle with an angle A' that is $180^\circ - A$, which is not A). * **Case 2: Angle A is Obtuse ($A \ge 90^\circ$)** * **a $\le$ b:** No triangle exists. Side 'a' must be the longest side if it's opposite an obtuse angle. * **a > b:** Exactly one triangle. **How to Handle SSA Mathematically:** When using the Law of Sines for SSA (e.g., to find angle B when given a, b, A): $\frac{\sin B}{b} = \frac{\sin A}{a} \implies \sin B = \frac{b \sin A}{a}$ * If $\sin B > 1$, no solution (corresponds to `a < h`). * If $\sin B = 1$, one right-angle solution ($B = 90^\circ$, corresponds to `a = h`). * If $\sin B < 1$: * Calculate $B_1 = \arcsin\left(\frac{b \sin A}{a}\right)$. * Calculate $B_2 = 180^\circ - B_1$. * Check if $B_2$ is a valid angle (i.e., if $A + B_2 < 180^\circ$). If both $B_1$ and $B_2$ are valid, you have two triangles. This happens when `h < a < b`. ### 6. Examples **Example 1: AAS Case (Finding a side and an angle)** Given triangle ABC: A = 30°, B = 70°, and side a = 10 cm. Find angle C and side b. 1. **Find Angle C:** C = 180° - (A + B) = 180° - (30° + 70°) = 180° - 100° = 80°. 2. **Find Side b using Law of Sines:** $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B}$ $\frac{10}{\sin 30^\circ} = \frac{b}{\sin 70^\circ}$ $b = \frac{10 \cdot \sin 70^\circ}{\sin 30^\circ} = \frac{10 \cdot 0.9397}{0.5} \approx 18.794$ cm. **Example 2: SSA Ambiguous Case** Given triangle ABC: a = 6, b = 10, A = 30°. Find angle B. 1. **Use Law of Sines to find $\sin B$:** $\frac{\sin B}{b} = \frac{\sin A}{a}$ $\frac{\sin B}{10} = \frac{\sin 30^\circ}{6}$ $\sin B = \frac{10 \cdot \sin 30^\circ}{6} = \frac{10 \cdot 0.5}{6} = \frac{5}{6} \approx 0.8333$ 2. **Calculate possible values for B:** $B_1 = \arcsin(0.8333) \approx 56.44^\circ$ Since $\sin B$ is positive in both Quadrant I and II, there's a second possible angle: $B_2 = 180^\circ - B_1 = 180^\circ - 56.44^\circ = 123.56^\circ$ 3. **Check for valid triangles:** * **Triangle 1:** A = 30°, B1 = 56.44°. $A + B_1 = 30^\circ + 56.44^\circ = 86.44^\circ < 180^\circ$. This is a valid triangle. C1 = 180° - 86.44° = 93.56°. * **Triangle 2:** A = 30°, B2 = 123.56°. $A + B_2 = 30^\circ + 123.56^\circ = 153.56^\circ < 180^\circ$. This is also a valid triangle. C2 = 180° - 153.56° = 26.44°. In this case (a=6, b=10, A=30°), two possible triangles exist because $h = b \sin A = 10 \sin 30^\circ = 5$. Since $h < a < b$ ($5 < 6 < 10$), it's the ambiguous case with two solutions. ### 7. Advanced Connections & Interesting Points * **Relation to the Circumradius (R):** A less common but insightful extension of the Law of Sines is its relation to the circumradius (R) of the triangle (the radius of the circle that passes through all three vertices). $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C} = 2R$ This constant ratio for any triangle is equal to twice the circumradius. * **Area of a Triangle:** The area of a triangle can be expressed using the Law of Sines: Area $= \frac{1}{2}ab \sin C = \frac{1}{2}bc \sin A = \frac{1}{2}ac \sin B$. By substituting $\sin C = \frac{c \sin A}{a}$ (from the law of sines), you can derive other area formulas. * **Limitations:** The Law of Sines cannot be used directly if you are given: * SSS (Side-Side-Side): You need to use the Law of Cosines first to find an angle. * SAS (Side-Angle-Side): You need to use the Law of Cosines first to find the third side. * **Engineering Relevance:** The Law of Sines is crucial in fields like: * **Surveying and Navigation:** Determining distances and positions that are difficult to measure directly. * **Structural Engineering:** Analyzing forces and stresses in triangular trusses and frames. * **Robotics:** Calculating joint angles for manipulator arms. * **Physics:** Vector resolution and trajectory calculations. ### Summary of Key Points * The Law of Sines states $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$ for any triangle. * It's derived from the height of a triangle. * It's used to solve triangles given AAS, ASA, or SSA (the ambiguous case). * The SSA (Side-Side-Angle) case is "ambiguous" and may yield zero, one, or two possible triangles, depending on the lengths of the sides relative to the altitude from the known angle. * The ratio $\frac{a}{\sin A}$ (etc.) is equal to $2R$, where R is the circumradius of the triangle. * It's a foundational tool for solving oblique triangles in various engineering and scientific applications. 19.) Law of Cosines The Law of Cosines is a fundamental principle in trigonometry that extends the Pythagorean Theorem to all triangles, not just right-angled ones. It establishes a relationship between the lengths of the sides of a triangle and the cosine of one of its angles. ### 1. Definition and Purpose The Law of Cosines relates the lengths of the sides of a triangle to the cosine of one of its angles. It is particularly useful when: * You know two sides and the included angle (SAS) and want to find the third side. * You know all three sides (SSS) and want to find any of the angles. ### 2. The Formula For a triangle with sides *a*, *b*, *c* and angles *A*, *B*, *C* opposite those respective sides: * **To find side *a***: $a^2 = b^2 + c^2 - 2bc \cos(A)$ * **To find side *b***: $b^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2ac \cos(B)$ * **To find side *c***: $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(C)$ These formulas can also be rearranged to find an angle when all three sides are known: * **To find angle *A***: $\cos(A) = \frac{b^2 + c^2 - a^2}{2bc}$ * **To find angle *B***: $\cos(B) = \frac{a^2 + c^2 - b^2}{2ac}$ * **To find angle *C***: $\cos(C) = \frac{a^2 + b^2 - c^2}{2ab}$ ### 3. Derivation (Proof) Let's derive the formula for $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(C)$. Consider a triangle ABC. Place vertex C at the origin (0,0) of a coordinate plane. Let vertex B be on the positive x-axis at (a, 0). Let vertex A have coordinates $(x_A, y_A)$. Using trigonometry, we can express $x_A$ and $y_A$ in terms of side *b* and angle *C*: $x_A = b \cos(C)$ $y_A = b \sin(C)$ So, A is at $(b \cos(C), b \sin(C))$. Now, we use the distance formula to find the length of side *c* (the distance between A and B): $c^2 = (x_A - x_B)^2 + (y_A - y_B)^2$ $c^2 = (b \cos(C) - a)^2 + (b \sin(C) - 0)^2$ $c^2 = (b^2 \cos^2(C) - 2ab \cos(C) + a^2) + (b^2 \sin^2(C))$ $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 \cos^2(C) + b^2 \sin^2(C) - 2ab \cos(C)$ Factor out $b^2$: $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 (\cos^2(C) + \sin^2(C)) - 2ab \cos(C)$ Using the Pythagorean Identity $\sin^2(\theta) + \cos^2(\theta) = 1$: $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 (1) - 2ab \cos(C)$ $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(C)$ The other forms of the Law of Cosines can be derived similarly by placing different vertices at the origin. ### 4. Applications and Use Cases The Law of Cosines is indispensable in surveying, navigation, physics, and engineering for calculating unknown sides or angles in non-right triangles. * **Case 1: Side-Angle-Side (SAS) – Finding a Side** Given two sides and the included angle. *Example:* A triangle has sides $a = 10$ cm, $b = 15$ cm, and the included angle $C = 60^\circ$. Find side $c$. $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(C)$ $c^2 = 10^2 + 15^2 - 2(10)(15) \cos(60^\circ)$ $c^2 = 100 + 225 - 300(0.5)$ $c^2 = 325 - 150$ $c^2 = 175$ $c = \sqrt{175} \approx 13.23$ cm * **Case 2: Side-Side-Side (SSS) – Finding an Angle** Given all three sides. *Example:* A triangle has sides $a = 7$ m, $b = 9$ m, and $c = 12$ m. Find angle $A$. $\cos(A) = \frac{b^2 + c^2 - a^2}{2bc}$ $\cos(A) = \frac{9^2 + 12^2 - 7^2}{2(9)(12)}$ $\cos(A) = \frac{81 + 144 - 49}{216}$ $\cos(A) = \frac{176}{216} = \frac{22}{27}$ $A = \cos^{-1}(\frac{22}{27}) \approx 35.3^\circ$ ### 5. Comparison with Law of Sines * **Law of Sines**: $\frac{a}{\sin A} = \frac{b}{\sin B} = \frac{c}{\sin C}$ * **When to Use Law of Sines**: * ASA (Angle-Side-Angle) or AAS (Angle-Angle-Side): To find a side. * SSA (Side-Side-Angle): To find an angle (Caution: This case can be ambiguous, leading to 0, 1, or 2 possible triangles). * **When to Use Law of Cosines**: * SAS (Side-Angle-Side): To find a side. * SSS (Side-Side-Side): To find an angle. * **Key Difference**: The Law of Cosines always yields a unique solution for unknown sides/angles in the SAS and SSS cases, avoiding the ambiguity sometimes found with the Law of Sines in the SSA case. If you have the SSA case, it's often safer to use the Law of Cosines first to find the third side, then use Law of Sines for angles if needed, or stick to Law of Cosines. ### 6. Special Cases and Interesting Points * **Reduction to Pythagorean Theorem**: If the angle $C$ is a right angle ($90^\circ$), then $\cos(C) = \cos(90^\circ) = 0$. The formula $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab \cos(C)$ becomes: $c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - 2ab (0)$ $c^2 = a^2 + b^2$ This shows that the Pythagorean Theorem is simply a special case of the Law of Cosines when the triangle is a right-angled triangle. Hence, the Law of Cosines is often called the "generalized Pythagorean Theorem." * **Vector Interpretation**: The Law of Cosines can be derived using the dot product of vectors. If two vectors $\vec{u}$ and $\vec{v}$ originate from the same point, and the angle between them is $\theta$, then the magnitude of their difference vector $|\vec{u} - \vec{v}|$ corresponds to the third side of the triangle formed by $\vec{u}$, $\vec{v}$, and $\vec{u}-\vec{v}$. $|\vec{u} - \vec{v}|^2 = (\vec{u} - \vec{v}) \cdot (\vec{u} - \vec{v})$ $= \vec{u} \cdot \vec{u} - 2(\vec{u} \cdot \vec{v}) + \vec{v} \cdot \vec{v}$ Since $\vec{u} \cdot \vec{u} = |\vec{u}|^2$ and $\vec{u} \cdot \vec{v} = |\vec{u}| |\vec{v}| \cos(\theta)$, we get: $|\vec{u} - \vec{v}|^2 = |\vec{u}|^2 + |\vec{v}|^2 - 2|\vec{u}| |\vec{v}| \cos(\theta)$ This is exactly the Law of Cosines, where the sides are the magnitudes of the vectors and the angle is the angle between them. This highlights its broad applicability in physics and engineering, especially in vector analysis. ### Summary of Key Points * The Law of Cosines relates the sides and angles of *any* triangle. * It is a generalization of the Pythagorean Theorem. * **Formulas**: $a^2 = b^2 + c^2 - 2bc \cos(A)$ (and permutations), or $\cos(A) = \frac{b^2 + c^2 - a^2}{2bc}$ (and permutations). * **Primary Use Cases**: * Finding a side when two sides and the *included* angle (SAS) are known. * Finding an angle when all three sides (SSS) are known. * Unlike the Law of Sines in the SSA case, the Law of Cosines always yields a unique solution for these specific cases. * It has a direct connection to vector dot products, making it crucial in vector analysis. 20.) Area of Triangles using Trigonometry ## Area of Triangles Using Trigonometry The fundamental concept of calculating the area of a triangle, $A = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height}$, is well-known. However, in many real-world scenarios, the perpendicular height is not readily available or easily measurable. This is where trigonometry becomes an indispensable tool, allowing us to determine the area using combinations of sides and angles. ### 1. Prerequisites and Standard Notation Before diving into the derivations, let's establish the standard notation for a triangle: * **Vertices:** Denoted by capital letters A, B, C. * **Angles:** The angles at these vertices are also denoted by A, B, C (or $\alpha, \beta, \gamma$). * **Sides:** The side opposite vertex A is denoted by 'a', opposite B by 'b', and opposite C by 'c'. We will extensively use the **Sine function**, defined in a right-angled triangle as: * $\text{sin}(\text{angle}) = \frac{\text{Opposite Side}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$ ### 2. Derivation of the Basic Trigonometric Area Formula (SAS Formula) This is the most common trigonometric formula for the area of a triangle, used when two sides and the included angle (Side-Angle-Side, SAS) are known. **Proof/Derivation:** Consider a triangle ABC. Let's draw an altitude (height) 'h' from vertex B to side AC, meeting AC at point D. The area of triangle ABC using the basic formula is $A = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{b} \times \text{h}$. Now, let's relate 'h' to the known sides and angles using trigonometry. **Case 1: Angle A is Acute (A < 90°)** In the right-angled triangle ADB: $\text{sin}(A) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}} = \frac{h}{c}$ Therefore, $h = c \times \text{sin}(A)$. Substitute this value of 'h' back into the area formula: $A = \frac{1}{2} \times b \times (c \times \text{sin}(A))$ $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$ **Case 2: Angle A is Obtuse (A > 90°)** If angle A is obtuse, the altitude 'h' from B will fall outside the triangle, on the extension of AC. Let D be the foot of the perpendicular from B to the line containing AC. In the right-angled triangle ADB: The angle adjacent to A on the straight line is $(180^\circ - A)$. $\text{sin}(180^\circ - A) = \frac{h}{c}$ Since $\text{sin}(180^\circ - \theta) = \text{sin}(\theta)$, we have $\text{sin}(180^\circ - A) = \text{sin}(A)$. So, $h = c \times \text{sin}(A)$. Substituting 'h' back into the area formula ($A = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2} \times b \times h$): $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$ The formula holds true for both acute and obtuse angles. By drawing altitudes from different vertices, we can derive similar formulas for other combinations of sides and angles: * $A = \frac{1}{2} ab \text{sin}(C)$ (using sides 'a', 'b' and included angle C) * $A = \frac{1}{2} ca \text{sin}(B)$ (using sides 'c', 'a' and included angle B) **General Formula:** The area of a triangle is half the product of the lengths of two sides and the sine of the included angle. **Example 1: Calculating Area (SAS Case)** A triangular plot of land has two sides measuring 50 meters and 70 meters, with the angle between them being 60 degrees. Find the area of the plot. Given: $b = 50$ m, $c = 70$ m, $A = 60^\circ$. Using the formula $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$: $A = \frac{1}{2} \times 50 \times 70 \times \text{sin}(60^\circ)$ $A = \frac{1}{2} \times 3500 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ $A = 1750 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} = 875\sqrt{3}$ square meters. $A \approx 875 \times 1.732 \approx 1515.5$ square meters. ### 3. Area Formula Using the Law of Sines (AAS or ASA Formula) Sometimes, we know one side and two angles (Angle-Angle-Side, AAS, or Angle-Side-Angle, ASA). In such cases, we can use the Law of Sines to adapt the basic area formula. The Law of Sines states: $\frac{a}{\text{sin}(A)} = \frac{b}{\text{sin}(B)} = \frac{c}{\text{sin}(C)} = 2R$ (where R is the circumradius). Let's start with $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$. From the Law of Sines: $\frac{b}{\text{sin}(B)} = \frac{c}{\text{sin}(C)} \Rightarrow b = \frac{c \text{sin}(B)}{\text{sin}(C)}$ Also, $\frac{b}{\text{sin}(B)} = \frac{a}{\text{sin}(A)} \Rightarrow b = \frac{a \text{sin}(B)}{\text{sin}(A)}$ And $\frac{c}{\text{sin}(C)} = \frac{a}{\text{sin}(A)} \Rightarrow c = \frac{a \text{sin}(C)}{\text{sin}(A)}$ Substitute the expression for 'b' and 'c' (in terms of 'a', 'A', 'B', 'C') into the area formula: $A = \frac{1}{2} \left(\frac{a \text{sin}(B)}{\text{sin}(A)}\right) \left(\frac{a \text{sin}(C)}{\text{sin}(A)}\right) \text{sin}(A)$ $A = \frac{1}{2} \frac{a^2 \text{sin}(B) \text{sin}(C)}{\text{sin}^2(A)} \text{sin}(A)$ $A = \frac{a^2 \text{sin}(B) \text{sin}(C)}{2 \text{sin}(A)}$ Since $A+B+C = 180^\circ$, we know $A = 180^\circ - (B+C)$, so $\text{sin}(A) = \text{sin}(180^\circ - (B+C)) = \text{sin}(B+C)$. Thus, the formula can also be written as: $A = \frac{a^2 \text{sin}(B) \text{sin}(C)}{2 \text{sin}(B+C)}$ Similarly, we can derive other forms: * $A = \frac{b^2 \text{sin}(A) \text{sin}(C)}{2 \text{sin}(B)}$ or $A = \frac{b^2 \text{sin}(A) \text{sin}(C)}{2 \text{sin}(A+C)}$ * $A = \frac{c^2 \text{sin}(A) \text{sin}(B)}{2 \text{sin}(C)}$ or $A = \frac{c^2 \text{sin}(A) \text{sin}(B)}{2 \text{sin}(A+B)}$ **Example 2: Calculating Area (AAS Case)** A triangle has a side of length 10 cm. The angles adjacent to this side are 45° and 75°. Find its area. Given: $c = 10$ cm, $A = 45^\circ$, $B = 75^\circ$. First, find the third angle: $C = 180^\circ - (45^\circ + 75^\circ) = 180^\circ - 120^\circ = 60^\circ$. Using the formula $A = \frac{c^2 \text{sin}(A) \text{sin}(B)}{2 \text{sin}(C)}$: $A = \frac{10^2 \times \text{sin}(45^\circ) \times \text{sin}(75^\circ)}{2 \times \text{sin}(60^\circ)}$ $A = \frac{100 \times \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \times \text{sin}(75^\circ)}{2 \times \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}}$ $A = \frac{100 \times \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \times \left(\frac{\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{2}}{4}\right)}{\sqrt{3}}$ $A = \frac{50\sqrt{2} \times \left(\frac{\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{2}}{4}\right)}{\sqrt{3}}$ $A = \frac{25\sqrt{2}(\sqrt{6}+\sqrt{2})}{2\sqrt{3}} = \frac{25(\sqrt{12}+2)}{2\sqrt{3}} = \frac{25(2\sqrt{3}+2)}{2\sqrt{3}} = \frac{25 \times 2(\sqrt{3}+1)}{2\sqrt{3}}$ $A = 25 \frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{\sqrt{3}} = 25 \left(1 + \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}\right) = 25 \left(1 + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}\right) \text{ cm}^2$ $A \approx 25 (1 + 0.577) \approx 25 \times 1.577 \approx 39.425 \text{ cm}^2$. ### 4. Area Formulas Involving Inradius (r) and Circumradius (R) These formulas provide powerful connections between a triangle's area and the radii of its inscribed and circumscribed circles. #### a) Area in terms of Inradius (r) The **inradius** is the radius of the circle inscribed within the triangle (tangent to all three sides). **Definition:** Let 'r' be the inradius and 's' be the semi-perimeter of the triangle ($s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$). **Formula:** $A = rs$ **Proof/Derivation (Geometric):** Connect the incenter (center of the inscribed circle) to each vertex, forming three smaller triangles: $\triangle IBC$, $\triangle ICA$, and $\triangle IAB$. The height of each small triangle from the incenter to its base (which is a side of the main triangle) is 'r' (the inradius, as the tangent is perpendicular to the radius). Area($\triangle ABC$) = Area($\triangle IBC$) + Area($\triangle ICA$) + Area($\triangle IAB$) $A = \frac{1}{2} \times a \times r + \frac{1}{2} \times b \times r + \frac{1}{2} \times c \times r$ $A = \frac{1}{2} r (a+b+c)$ $A = r \left(\frac{a+b+c}{2}\right)$ $A = rs$ #### b) Area in terms of Circumradius (R) The **circumradius** is the radius of the circle that passes through all three vertices of the triangle. **Definition:** Let 'R' be the circumradius. **Formula:** $A = \frac{abc}{4R}$ **Proof/Derivation (using Law of Sines):** We know $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$. From the Law of Sines, we also have $\frac{a}{\text{sin}(A)} = 2R$. Therefore, $\text{sin}(A) = \frac{a}{2R}$. Substitute this into the area formula: $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \left(\frac{a}{2R}\right)$ $A = \frac{abc}{4R}$ **Example 3: Using Circumradius** A triangle has sides 6 cm, 8 cm, and 10 cm. Find its area and circumradius. This is a right-angled triangle (since $6^2 + 8^2 = 36 + 64 = 100 = 10^2$). Area $A = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2} \times 6 \times 8 = 24 \text{ cm}^2$. For a right-angled triangle, the circumradius R is half the hypotenuse. So, $R = \frac{10}{2} = 5$ cm. Let's verify with the formula $A = \frac{abc}{4R}$: $24 = \frac{6 \times 8 \times 10}{4 \times 5} = \frac{480}{20} = 24$. The formula holds. ### 5. Connection to Heron's Formula (SSC Formula) Heron's Formula calculates the area of a triangle when all three sides (Side-Side-Side, SSS) are known. **Formula:** $A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$, where $s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$ is the semi-perimeter. **How it relates to trigonometry:** Heron's Formula can be derived directly from the trigonometric area formula $A = \frac{1}{2} bc \text{sin}(A)$ combined with the Law of Cosines ($\text{cos}(A) = \frac{b^2+c^2-a^2}{2bc}$) and the identity $\text{sin}^2(A) = 1 - \text{cos}^2(A)$. Squaring the area formula: $A^2 = \frac{1}{4} b^2 c^2 \text{sin}^2(A)$ $A^2 = \frac{1}{4} b^2 c^2 (1 - \text{cos}^2(A))$ Substitute $\text{cos}(A)$: $A^2 = \frac{1}{4} b^2 c^2 \left(1 - \left(\frac{b^2+c^2-a^2}{2bc}\right)^2\right)$ $A^2 = \frac{1}{4} b^2 c^2 \left(\frac{(2bc)^2 - (b^2+c^2-a^2)^2}{(2bc)^2}\right)$ $A^2 = \frac{1}{16} \left((2bc)^2 - (b^2+c^2-a^2)^2\right)$ This expression, after applying the difference of squares factorization ($X^2 - Y^2 = (X-Y)(X+Y)$) and algebraic manipulation, leads directly to Heron's formula. This shows that Heron's formula is fundamentally rooted in trigonometric relationships. ### 6. Extra Interesting Points and Applications * **Vector Cross Product:** For engineering students, it's worth noting that the magnitude of the cross product of two vectors representing two sides of a triangle is twice the area of the triangle. If $\vec{u}$ and $\vec{v}$ are vectors representing two sides of a triangle originating from a common vertex, then the Area $= \frac{1}{2} |\vec{u} \times \vec{v}|$. This is because $|\vec{u} \times \vec{v}| = |\vec{u}| |\vec{v}| \text{sin}(\theta)$, where $\theta$ is the angle between them, directly mirroring the trigonometric area formula. This concept is crucial in physics and engineering for calculating torque, magnetic forces, etc. * **Surveying and Land Measurement:** Trigonometric area formulas are fundamental for surveyors to calculate the area of land parcels, especially when direct linear measurements for height are impractical due to obstacles or irregular shapes. * **Engineering Design:** In structural analysis, triangular elements are often used in finite element methods. Calculating the area of these elements (often defined by coordinates) is a preliminary step, and trigonometric methods can be used or implied. * **Navigation:** Calculating distances and areas in navigation (e.g., using spherical triangles on a globe) relies heavily on advanced trigonometric principles. ### Summary of Key Points * The basic area formula $A = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height}$ is extended using trigonometry when height is not directly known. * **SAS Formula:** $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\text{sin}(C) = \frac{1}{2}bc\text{sin}(A) = \frac{1}{2}ca\text{sin}(B)$. This is the most direct application. * **AAS/ASA Formula:** Derived using the Law of Sines, e.g., $A = \frac{a^2 \text{sin}(B) \text{sin}(C)}{2 \text{sin}(A)}$. Useful when one side and two angles are known. * **Inradius Formula:** $A = rs$, where 'r' is the inradius and 's' is the semi-perimeter. Useful for problems involving inscribed circles. * **Circumradius Formula:** $A = \frac{abc}{4R}$, where 'R' is the circumradius. Useful for problems involving circumscribed circles. * Heron's Formula is an alternative for SSS cases and can be derived from the trigonometric area formula. * These formulas are widely applied in engineering, surveying, and physics due to their ability to connect geometric properties with angles and lengths. 21.) Solving Trigonometric Equations ## Solving Trigonometric Equations Solving trigonometric equations involves finding the values of the variable (usually an angle) that satisfy a given equation containing trigonometric functions. Unlike algebraic equations that yield specific numerical solutions, trigonometric equations often have infinitely many solutions due due to the periodic nature of trigonometric functions. ### 1. Fundamental Concepts & Definitions * **Trigonometric Equation:** An equation that involves trigonometric functions of a variable. Examples: $\sin x = 0.5$, $2\cos^2 \theta - 3\cos \theta + 1 = 0$. * **Periodicity:** Trigonometric functions repeat their values at regular intervals. * $\sin(x + 2n\pi) = \sin x$ and $\cos(x + 2n\pi) = \cos x$ (period $2\pi$) * $\tan(x + n\pi) = \tan x$ (period $\pi$) Where $n$ is an integer. This is crucial for finding *general solutions*. * **Principal Solution:** The smallest positive or negative angle that satisfies the equation, usually within a specific range like $[0, 2\pi)$ or $(-\pi, \pi]$. Often derived from the inverse trigonometric functions. * **General Solution:** The set of all possible solutions for the equation, incorporating the periodic nature. * **Unit Circle:** An indispensable tool. It visually represents sine (y-coordinate), cosine (x-coordinate), and tangent (slope or y/x) for any angle, helping to identify angles with specific trigonometric values in different quadrants. * **Inverse Trigonometric Functions:** Functions like $\sin^{-1}(x)$, $\cos^{-1}(x)$, $\tan^{-1}(x)$ (also denoted as arcsin, arccos, arctan) provide the *principal value* (an angle) for a given trigonometric ratio. For example, $\sin^{-1}(0.5) = \pi/6$ or $30^\circ$. ### 2. General Solution Formulas These are derived directly from the periodicity and serve as the backbone for finding all solutions. For a principal solution $\alpha$: * If $\sin x = \sin \alpha \implies x = n\pi + (-1)^n \alpha$, where $n \in \mathbb{Z}$ (integers). * *Derivation Idea:* Consider the unit circle. $\sin x = \sin \alpha$ occurs when $x = \alpha$ or $x = \pi - \alpha$. Adding $2n\pi$ covers all periods. The $(-1)^n$ term elegantly combines both cases. * If $\cos x = \cos \alpha \implies x = 2n\pi \pm \alpha$, where $n \in \mathbb{Z}$. * *Derivation Idea:* $\cos x = \cos \alpha$ occurs when $x = \alpha$ or $x = -\alpha$. Adding $2n\pi$ covers all periods. * If $\tan x = \tan \alpha \implies x = n\pi + \alpha$, where $n \in \mathbb{Z}$. * *Derivation Idea:* $\tan x = \tan \alpha$ occurs when $x = \alpha$. Due to $\tan$'s period of $\pi$, adding $n\pi$ covers all solutions. ### 3. Types of Trigonometric Equations and Solving Strategies #### 3.1 Type 1: Simple Equations ($\sin x = k$, $\cos x = k$, $\tan x = k$) * **Strategy:** Find the principal value $\alpha = \sin^{-1}(k)$ (or $\cos^{-1}(k)$, $\tan^{-1}(k)$) and then apply the general solution formulas. * **Example:** Solve $\sin x = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$. * The principal value is $\alpha = \sin^{-1}\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right) = \frac{\pi}{3}$. * Using the general formula: $x = n\pi + (-1)^n \frac{\pi}{3}$, $n \in \mathbb{Z}$. #### 3.2 Type 2: Equations Reducible to Simple Form (Using Identities) Many equations require trigonometric identities to simplify them into a basic form. * **Key Identities:** * **Pythagorean:** $\sin^2 x + \cos^2 x = 1$, $\sec^2 x - \tan^2 x = 1$, $\csc^2 x - \cot^2 x = 1$ * **Double Angle:** $\sin(2x) = 2\sin x \cos x$, $\cos(2x) = \cos^2 x - \sin^2 x = 2\cos^2 x - 1 = 1 - 2\sin^2 x$, $\tan(2x) = \frac{2\tan x}{1-\tan^2 x}$ * **Sum/Difference:** $\sin(A \pm B)$, $\cos(A \pm B)$, $\tan(A \pm B)$ * **Product-to-Sum/Sum-to-Product:** Useful for combining terms. * **Example:** Solve $2\cos^2 x - \sin x - 1 = 0$. * Use $\cos^2 x = 1 - \sin^2 x$: * $2(1 - \sin^2 x) - \sin x - 1 = 0$ * $2 - 2\sin^2 x - \sin x - 1 = 0$ * $-2\sin^2 x - \sin x + 1 = 0$ * $2\sin^2 x + \sin x - 1 = 0$ (This is a quadratic in $\sin x$) #### 3.3 Type 3: Quadratic Form (after simplification) * **Strategy:** Substitute a variable (e.g., $y = \sin x$), solve the quadratic equation for $y$, then solve for $x$ using the basic general solution formulas. Factoring or the quadratic formula ($y = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$) can be used. * **Continuing Example (from 3.2):** Solve $2\sin^2 x + \sin x - 1 = 0$. * Let $y = \sin x$. $2y^2 + y - 1 = 0$. * Factoring: $(2y - 1)(y + 1) = 0$. * So, $2y - 1 = 0 \implies y = 1/2$ or $y + 1 = 0 \implies y = -1$. * Case 1: $\sin x = 1/2$. Principal $\alpha = \pi/6$. General $x = n\pi + (-1)^n \frac{\pi}{6}$. * Case 2: $\sin x = -1$. Principal $\alpha = -\pi/2$. General $x = n\pi + (-1)^n (-\frac{\pi}{2})$. #### 3.4 Type 4: Homogeneous Equations ($a\sin x + b\cos x = 0$ or $a\sin^2 x + b\sin x \cos x + c\cos^2 x = 0$) * **Strategy:** Divide by the highest power of $\cos x$ to convert the equation into terms of $\tan x$. (Ensure $\cos x \neq 0$ is not a solution, or handle that case separately). * **Example:** Solve $\sqrt{3}\sin x + \cos x = 0$. * Divide by $\cos x$ (assuming $\cos x \neq 0$. If $\cos x = 0$, then $\sin x = \pm 1$, but this doesn't satisfy the equation, so $\cos x \neq 0$ is a valid assumption). * $\sqrt{3}\frac{\sin x}{\cos x} + \frac{\cos x}{\cos x} = 0$ * $\sqrt{3}\tan x + 1 = 0 \implies \tan x = -\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}$. * Principal $\alpha = -\frac{\pi}{6}$. General $x = n\pi - \frac{\pi}{6}$, $n \in \mathbb{Z}$. #### 3.5 Type 5: Equations with Multiple Angles ($\sin(nx) = k$, $\cos(nx) = k$, etc.) * **Strategy:** Let $y = nx$. Solve for $y$ using general solution formulas, then solve for $x$. Remember that for a given range of $x$, $nx$ will span a larger range. * **Example:** Solve $\cos(2x) = -\frac{1}{2}$ for $x \in [0, 2\pi)$. * Let $y = 2x$. We need to solve $\cos y = -\frac{1}{2}$. * Principal values for $y$ are $\frac{2\pi}{3}$ and $\frac{4\pi}{3}$. * General solution for $y$: $y = 2n\pi \pm \frac{2\pi}{3}$. * Substitute back $2x$: $2x = 2n\pi \pm \frac{2\pi}{3}$. * Divide by 2: $x = n\pi \pm \frac{\pi}{3}$. * Now, find specific solutions within $[0, 2\pi)$: * $n=0: x = \pi/3, -\pi/3$ (reject $-\pi/3$) * $n=1: x = \pi + \pi/3 = 4\pi/3, \pi - \pi/3 = 2\pi/3$ * $n=2: x = 2\pi + \pi/3$ (reject, outside range), $2\pi - \pi/3 = 5\pi/3$ * Solutions in $[0, 2\pi)$ are $\frac{\pi}{3}, \frac{2\pi}{3}, \frac{4\pi}{3}, \frac{5\pi}{3}$. #### 3.6 Type 6: Equations of the form $a \sin x + b \cos x = c$ * **Strategy:** Convert the left side into a single trigonometric function using the **R-form** (also called auxiliary angle method or R-formula). * $a \sin x + b \cos x = R \sin(x + \alpha)$ or $R \cos(x - \alpha)$ * Where $R = \sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$, and $\alpha$ is found from $\tan \alpha = b/a$ (for $R \sin(x+\alpha)$ form, $\alpha$ is the angle whose terminal arm passes through $(a,b)$ or $(b,a)$ depending on the chosen identity. For $R\cos(x-\alpha)$, $\alpha$ is given by $a = R \cos \alpha$ and $b = R \sin \alpha$). * **Derivation (for $R \cos(x-\alpha)$):** * $R \cos(x-\alpha) = R (\cos x \cos \alpha + \sin x \sin \alpha)$ * $R \cos(x-\alpha) = (R \cos \alpha)\cos x + (R \sin \alpha)\sin x$ * Comparing with $b \cos x + a \sin x$: * $b = R \cos \alpha$ * $a = R \sin \alpha$ * Squaring and adding: $a^2 + b^2 = R^2 \sin^2 \alpha + R^2 \cos^2 \alpha = R^2(\sin^2 \alpha + \cos^2 \alpha) = R^2 \implies R = \sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$. * Dividing: $\frac{R \sin \alpha}{R \cos \alpha} = \frac{a}{b} \implies \tan \alpha = \frac{a}{b}$. The quadrant of $\alpha$ is determined by the signs of $a$ and $b$. * **Example:** Solve $\sin x + \cos x = 1$. * Here $a=1, b=1$. $R = \sqrt{1^2 + 1^2} = \sqrt{2}$. * We want $R \cos(x - \alpha) = \sqrt{2} \cos(x - \alpha) = \sin x + \cos x$. * $1 = \sqrt{2} \sin \alpha \implies \sin \alpha = 1/\sqrt{2}$ * $1 = \sqrt{2} \cos \alpha \implies \cos \alpha = 1/\sqrt{2}$ * So, $\alpha = \pi/4$. * The equation becomes $\sqrt{2} \cos(x - \frac{\pi}{4}) = 1$. * $\cos(x - \frac{\pi}{4}) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}$. * Let $y = x - \frac{\pi}{4}$. $\cos y = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}$. * Principal $\alpha' = \pi/4$. * General solution for $y$: $y = 2n\pi \pm \frac{\pi}{4}$. * Substitute back: $x - \frac{\pi}{4} = 2n\pi \pm \frac{\pi}{4}$. * Case 1: $x - \frac{\pi}{4} = 2n\pi + \frac{\pi}{4} \implies x = 2n\pi + \frac{\pi}{2}$. * Case 2: $x - \frac{\pi}{4} = 2n\pi - \frac{\pi}{4} \implies x = 2n\pi$. ### 4. Important Considerations & Extra Points * **Domain and Range:** Be mindful of the domain of the variable (e.g., $x \in [0, 2\pi)$ or all real numbers) and the range of trigonometric functions ($[-1, 1]$ for sine/cosine). If $\sin x = 2$, there's no solution. * **Extraneous Solutions:** Squaring both sides of an equation (e.g., to use $\sin^2 x + \cos^2 x = 1$) can introduce extraneous solutions.
